The Two Types of Parts: Material and Formal
Bannani notes that the part of a compound can be material or formal, defining the material part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the material part of any compound expression and distinguish it from the formal part.

Core Idea
A compound word has two kinds of parts: the lexical stuff and the structural relation.

In logical and grammatical analysis, when we speak of the 'parts' of a compound expression, we must be careful: these parts can be material or formal. This lesson clarifies what the material part is, using a concrete example from Arabic grammar. Understanding this distinction will sharpen your analysis of definitions in logic and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Find the main claim
Bannani states that the part is of two kinds: material and formal. Notice that he sets up a division (qismani) for the reader.
2
Understand the definition of material
He defines the material part as 'the very substance of the utterance'. This means the actual words themselves, not their meanings or relations.
3
Apply to the example
For the compound 'غلام زيد', the material parts are the words 'غلام' and 'زيد'. Verify that these are the lexical units composing the phrase.
4
Contrast with formal
The formal part, though not defined here, is the syntactic relation (iḍāfa) that links the words. Without it, the words remain isolated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيهان (۱) - الأوَّلُ : اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الجُزْءَ قِسْمَانِ : مَادِيٌّ ، وَصُورِيٌّ . فَالمَادِيُّ هُوَ : «نَفْسُ جَوْهَرِ اللَّفْظِ كَ: غُلَامٍ وَزَيْدٍ مِنْ غُلَامُ زَيْدٍ».
Two notes. The first: Know that the part is of two kinds: material and formal. The material is: the very substance of the utterance, like 'غلام' and 'زيد' from 'غلام زيد'.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is a brief definitional note within the commentary. Bannani alerts the reader that the 'part' (جزء) mentioned in definitions of compound and single words can be understood in two ways: material and formal. The material part is the actual lexical components that make up the utterance, such as the words 'غلام' (boy) and 'زيد' (Zaid) in the compound phrase 'غلام زيد' (Zaid's boy). The formal part, which is implied but not defined here, is the relational structure or syntactic arrangement that gives the composition its meaning. This distinction is important for understanding what is meant by 'part' in logical definitions.

Key Terms
مَادِي
صُورِي
جُزْء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the compound phrase 'غلام زيد', what is the material part according to Bannani's first note?
A) The possessive relation.
B) The words 'غلام' and 'زيد'.
C) The meaning 'boy of Zaid'.
D) The entire phrase.

Key Takeaway
Every compound has two types of parts: the material (the words themselves) and the formal (the structure connecting them).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Formal Part: Definition and Non-Consideration
Definition of formal part and its non-consideration in composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the formal part of a compound and explain why it is not considered a real component in determining whether an expression is compound.

Core Idea
The formal part is an accidental structure, not a component; only material parts count for composition.

In logic and Arabic grammar, words are analyzed into material and formal parts. The formal part, like the verb pattern or vowel tones, is the shape that arises from composition. But is it a real part? This lesson examines the debate and the preferred view, using the verb 'qāma' as a key example.

Guided Path
1
Define the formal part
Find the definition of 'ṣūrī' in the Arabic. It is 'the accidental structure that comes to the word by composition'. The analogy given is a bed: the wood is material, the arrangement is formal.
The formal part is not a separate audible unit; it is the pattern or structure.
2
Distinguish material and formal
Material parts are audible letters or morphemes, which can be explicit or implicit (like hidden pronouns). The formal part is the accidental structure (hay'a) that results from arranging the material parts.
Implicit pronouns like the subject in 'aqūmu' are still considered material.
3
Identify the disagreement
Scholars differed on whether the formal part counts as a component of a compound. What are the two positions? One says it does count; the correct view says it does not.
The text explicitly states 'al-ṣaḥīḥ: 'adam i'tibārihi'.
4
Analyze the evidence for non-consideration
The verb 'qāma' (he stood) is considered single (mufrad) even though it has a form (ṣīgha). If the form were a part, 'qāma' would have two parts (material + formal) and thus be compound. Since grammarians treat it as single, the form is not a real part.
This shows that only material components determine composition.
5
Evaluate the objection and response
An objector argues that vowel movements (fatḥa, kasra, ḍamma) are estimated by letters (alif, yā', wāw) and are therefore heard sounds that should be parts. The commentator responds: even if estimated, the structure is still not considered a real part; otherwise, verbs would be compound, which no one holds.
The objection is based on Sībawayh's view, but it does not overturn the logical principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالصُّورِيُّ هُوَ : الهَيْئَةُ العَارِضَةُ لَهُ بِالتَّرْكِيبِ، وَنَظِيرُهُمَا فِي السَّرِيرِ خَشَبُهُ، وَصُورَةُ تَألِيفِهِ. ثُمَّ الجُزْءُ المَادِيُّ قَدْ يَكُونُ مُقَدَّراً فِي اللَّفْظِ كَالصَّمَائِرِ المُسْتَتِرَةِ، فَإِنَّهَا مِنْ قَبِيل المَادِي عِنْدَهُمْ، وَاخْتَلَفُوا فِي الصُّورِيِّ هَلْ يُعْتَبَرُ فِي المُرَكَبِ أَوْ لَا يُعْتَبَرُ، وَالصَّحِيحُ : عَدَمُ اعْتِبَارِهِ، وَلِذَا قَالُوا فِي الفِعْلِ نَحْو: «قَامَ أَنَّهُ مِنَ الْمُفْرَدِ؛ لِعَدَمِ اعْتِبَارِهِمْ جُزْئِهِ الصُّوْرِيّ الَّذِي هُوَ الصَّيْغَةُ، وَإِلَّا لَكَانَ مُرَكَّباً ، لأَنَّ فِيهِ جُزْأَيْنِ المَادَّةُ وَالصَّيْغَةُ. قَصَارَة. قوله : (العَارِضَةُ لَهُ أي : وليست جزء اللَّفظ، فالأجزاء لا تكون إلَّا ماديَّةٌ . قوله : (قَدْ يَكُونُ مُقَدَّراً) ردَّ به ما ذكره بعضهم من أنَّ المادي هو ما يُسمع، والصُّوري يسمع ; لأنه ظاهر القصور ; لاقتضائه أنَّ نحو: «أقوم» ليس بمركب عند من يشترط كون الأجزاء كلَّها ماديَّةٌ ; لكون الضَّمير لا يسمع، وهو باطل. قوله : (وَالصَّحِيحُ : عَدَمُ اعْتِبَارِهِ :أي: لأنَّه الهيئة العارضة والهيئةُ أمر اعتباري لا مدخل لها فى التركيب، فالأجزاء لا تكون إلا ماديَّةٌ ; قاله السَّيِّد في حواشي الرَّضي . واعترضه بعضهم : بأنَّ الهيئة الصُّوريَّة لها دخل في التركيب; لأنَّ الحركات المرتبة التي حصلت هيئة للفظ مقدرة بحروف، فالفتحة مقدرة بالألف، والكسرة بالياء، والضَّمُّة بالواو، فهي إذا أصوات منطوق بها، فلها دخل في الجزئية، واللفظ بسببها يحكم عليها بالتركيب; قال: وممَّن نصَّ على أنَّ الحركات مقدرة بالحروف سيبويه . اهـ . قلت : ما ذكره من أنَّ الهيئة مقدَّرة بالحروف ولها دخل في الجزئية لا يقتضي أن تكون الهيئة عندهم معتبرة في تسمية المركَّب مركَّباً، فالهيئة وإن كانت داخلة غير معتبرة; بدليل أنَّهم قالوا في الفعل إنَّه مفرد ; لعدم اعتبارهم جزئه الصُّوري، وعلى ما قاله المعترض يلزم أن يكون الفعل مركَّباً، ولم يقل به أحدٌ. نعم; قول السيد : لأنَّ الهيئة أمر اعتباري أي : ليست ألفاظاً مسموعة غير مسلم; بدليل ما تقدَّم . قوله : (الَّذِي هُوَ الصَّيْغَةُ أعني : الصُّورة الحاصلة للحروف باعتبار التَّقديم والتأخير، والحركات
And the formal (part) is: the accidental structure that comes to it by composition, and their analogy in a bed is its wood and the form of its assembly. Then the material part may be implicit in the utterance, like the hidden pronouns, for they are of the material type according to them. And they differed concerning the formal (part): whether it is considered in the compound or not considered. The correct (view) is: it is not considered. For this reason they said about the verb like 'qāma' (he stood) that it is single, due to their non-consideration of its formal part which is the form, otherwise it would be compound, because it would have two parts: material and formal. Commentary: His statement: 'the accidental to it' means: it is not a part of the utterance, for parts are only material. His statement: 'may be implicit' refutes what some mentioned: that the material is what is heard, and the formal is heard; but that is manifestly deficient because it entails that 'aqūmu' (I stand) is not compound according to those who stipulate that all parts are material, since the pronoun is not heard, which is false. His statement: 'the correct is its non-consideration' means: because it is the accidental structure, and the structure is a mental consideration having no entry into composition, so parts are only material; this is what al-Sayyid said in his glosses on al-Raḍī. Someone objected: that the formal structure has an entry into composition, because the ordered movements that form a structure for the utterance are estimated by letters: the fatḥa is estimated by an alif, the kasra by a yā', and the ḍamma by a wāw; thus they are actually uttered sounds, so they have entry in part-hood, and due to them the utterance is judged as compound. He said: among those who stated that movements are estimated by letters is Sībawayh. End. I say: what he mentioned—that the structure is estimated by letters and has entry in part-hood—does not necessitate that the structure is considered by them in naming a compound as compound. So the structure, even if internal, is not considered; evidence is that they said the verb is single due to their non-consideration of its formal part. According to what the objector said, it would be necessary that the verb is compound, and no one has said it. Yes, the statement of al-Sayyid—'because the structure is a mental consideration', meaning it is not heard utterances—is not accepted, due to what preceded. His statement: 'which is the form'—I mean the form obtained for the letters in terms of precedence and delay, and the movements
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Sullam defines the formal part (الصُّوري) of a compound expression and clarifies its status. The author begins by defining the formal part as the accidental structure that arises from composition, giving the analogy of a bed: the wood is the material, and the way it is assembled is the form. Then he discusses the material part, noting that it can be implicit, like hidden pronouns in verbs such as 'aqūmu', which are still considered material parts. The main issue is whether the formal part (like the verb form or Arabic vowel markings) is counted as a component in determining whether an expression is compound. The correct view is that it is not considered, because it is only an accidental structure, not a real part. Evidence: verbs like 'qāma' are treated as single words (not compound) even though they have a form (صيغة) in addition to the material letters; if the form were considered a part, the verb would be two-part and hence compound. The commentary then explains each phrase of the main text. An objection is raised: the movements (fatḥa, kasra, ḍamma) are actually estimated by letters (alif, yā', wāw) and thus are heard sounds that contribute to part-hood—citing Sībawayh. The commentator responds: even if the structure is estimated by letters and has entry in part-hood, it does not mean it is considered a real part; the fact that grammarians treat the verb as single shows they do not count the form. The commentator also critiques al-Sayyid's claim that the structure is purely mental, noting that it is not accepted because the structure does have some external manifestation. This lesson establishes a key principle: only material components (letters, morphemes) are real parts; the formal pattern is an attribute, not a component.

Key Terms
صوري
مادي
هيئة
صيغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the correct view regarding the formal part (الصُّوري) in determining whether an expression is compound?
A) It is considered a part, so verbs like 'qāma' are compound.
B) It is not considered a part, so verbs like 'qāma' are single.
C) It is considered only if it is implicit like hidden pronouns.
D) It is considered according to Sībawayh's opinion.

Key Takeaway
The formal part of a compound is an accidental structure, not a material component, and therefore is not counted in determining whether an expression is compound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the word 'يَضْرِبُ' (yadribu, he hits) by identifying its material letters and its formal pattern. According to the lesson, is this word considered single or compound? Why?

Conceptual vs. Ontological Priority in Definitions
Author argues that compound is defined before single because its concept is positive and single's is negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish conceptual priority from ontological priority and explain why compound is defined before single in definitions.

Core Idea
Conceptual priority can reverse ontological priority: the positive whole precedes the negative part in definition.

In Islamic logic, the order of definitions is not arbitrary. The author of al-Sullam deliberately defines compound before single, even though the single is ontologically prior as a part. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind that choice and introduces the crucial distinction between concept (mafhūm) and essence (dhāt), which is vital for correct definition in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of concepts
Notice that compound (murakkab) is a positive concept (wujūdī) because it involves composition, while single (mufrad) is a privative concept (ʿadamī) because it is defined by the absence of composition.
Positive concepts affirm something; privative concepts negate something.
2
Understand the priority principle
The text states: 'the negation of a thing is not understood except after comprehension of the negated thing.' Since the single is defined by negation (lack of composition), the compound's positive concept must be understood first.
This is a conceptual, not existential, priority.
3
Consider the objection
An objector argues: the single is a part of the compound, and parts are prior to the whole. So how can the part be defined after the whole? This is an ontological argument.
Ontological priority: the part exists before the whole.
4
Distinguish between essence and concept
The response distinguishes priority in essence (dhāt / maṣdaq) from priority in concept (mafhūm). In essence, the part is prior and necessary for the whole, but in concept, the positive whole is prior because the privative part depends on it.
The same thing can be prior in one sense and posterior in another.
5
Apply the distinction to the ordering
In definition, we are concerned with concepts. Therefore, compound is defined before single because its concept is positive and foundational for understanding the privative single.
This principle applies whenever a positive-privative pair appears.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في مباحث الألفاظ. الثَّانِي : قَدَّمَ النَّاظِمُ المُرَكَّبَ وَتَعْرِيفَهُ عَلَى المُفْرَد; لأَنَّ التَّعْرِيفَ لِلمَفْهُوم، وَمَنْهُومُ المُرَكَّبِ وُجُودِيٌّ ، وَمَنْهُومُ المُفْرَدِ عَدَمِيٌّ ; لأَنَّهُ سُلِبَ فِيهِ مَا أُثْبِتَ فِي المُرَكَّبِ، وَلَا يُعْقَلُ سَلْبُ أَمْرٍ إِلَّا بَعْدَ تَعَقُلِ الأَمْرِ المَسْلُوبِ. لا يُقَالُ : المُفْرَدُ جُزْءُ المُرَكَّب، وَالجُزْءُ سَابِقٌ عَلَى الكُلِّ ، وَأَيْضاً : الجُزْءُ لَازِمٌ لِلْكُلِّ ضَرُورَةٌ، فَكَيْفَ يُجْعَلُ قَسِيماً لَهُ، وَقَسِيمُ الشَّيْء يُبَاينه لِأَنَّا نَقُولُ: كَوْنُهُ جُزْءاً مِنَ المُرَكَبِ وَلَازماً لَهُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ بِحَسَبِ ذَاتِهِمَا ; أَيْ : مَصْدُوقِهِمَا ، وَذَلِكَ لَا يُنَافِي التَّقَابُلَ بَيْنَهُمَا ، وَالتَّبايُنُ بِحَسَبِ مَفْهُومِهِمَا المُقْتَضِي تَأخُرَ ذِكْرِ المُفْرَدِ عَنِ المُرَكَّبِ، فَافْهَمْ. قوله : (وَلَا يُعْقَلُ سَلْبُ أَمْرٍ ... إلخ) أي : لأنَّ السَّلب حكم، والحكم على الشَّيء فرع تصوره بوجه ما ولا يُنافي هذا كون القضيَّة السَّلبيَّة تصدق، وإن لم يوجد موضوعها; إذ لا يلزم من عدم وجوده عدم تعقله ; انظر: «اليوسي)) . قوله : (لَا يُقَالُ حاصل السُّؤال : أنَّه يلزم على ما درج عليه النَّاظم أمران: الدور، وكون قسم الشَّيء يُباينُهُ
Chapter on topics of expressions. Second: The author presented the compound and its definition before the single, because definition is for the concept, and the concept of the compound is positive, and the concept of the single is negative; because in it is negated what is affirmed in the compound, and the negation of a thing is not understood except after comprehension of the negated thing. It cannot be said: The single is a part of the compound, and the part is prior to the whole, and also the part is necessary for the whole necessarily, so how can it be made a counterpart to it, and the counterpart of a thing is opposite to it? Because we say: Its being a part of the compound and necessary for it is only in terms of their essences, i.e., their extensions, and that does not contradict the opposition between them, and the opposition is in terms of their concepts which necessitate that the mention of the single be later than the compound, so understand. Commentary: His statement: 'And the negation of a thing is not understood except after comprehension of the negated thing' etc., means: because negation is a judgment, and a judgment about something is a branch of its conception in some way, and this does not contradict the fact that the negative proposition can be true even if its subject does not exist; for the absence of its existence does not require its non-conception. See al-Yūsī. His statement: 'It cannot be said'—the summary of the objection is that what the author proceeded upon necessitates two things: circularity, and that the division of a thing is opposite to it.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage from the commentary begins a new chapter on topics of expressions. The author explains why in the text al-Sullam, the definition of compound (مركب) is given before that of single (مفرد). The reason is definitional priority: definition concerns the concept (مفهوم), and the concept of compound is positive (وجودي) because it involves a composition, while the concept of single is negative (عدمي) because it is defined by the absence of composition. Since negation of a thing presupposes understanding that thing, the positive concept must come first. An objection is raised: the single is a part of the compound, and parts are prior to the whole, so how can the part be defined after the whole? The response addresses that priority in terms of essence (ذات/مصدوق) is different from priority in concept (مفهوم). In essence, the part is prior and necessary for the whole, but in concept, the positive whole is prior because the negative part depends on it. The commentator then explains the phrase 'لا يعقل سلب أمر الا بعد تعقل الأمر المسلوب' (negation is not understood without prior comprehension), clarifying that it refers to conceptual priority, not existential. Even a negative proposition can be true about a non-existent subject; but the concept of that subject must still be understood. He also summarizes the objection as involving two issues: circularity (since if part is prior, why is whole defined first?) and that the division of a thing (qasim) should be opposite (mubayin) to it, but here the part is not opposite to the whole. The response is that opposition is in terms of concept, not essence. The lesson thus teaches an important methodological principle: conceptual priority may differ from ontological priority.

Key Terms
مفهوم
وجودي
عدمي
قسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author of al-Sullam define compound before single, according to this passage?
A) Because compound is more common in speech.
B) Because the concept of compound is positive and single's is negative, and negation presupposes affirmation.
C) Because the single is a part of the compound, and the whole is better known.
D) Because the author made a mistake in ordering.

Key Takeaway
The order of definitions depends on conceptual priority, not ontological priority; positive concepts precede privative ones because negation presupposes affirmation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a pair of concepts from uṣūl al-fiqh where one is positive and the other privative (e.g., wājib and mandūb, or 'āmm and khāṣṣ) and explain which should be defined first according to the principle taught.
?
Explain in one sentence why the single (mufrad) is defined after the compound in al-Sullam despite being its part.

How Verb Form Indicates Time: Objection and Answer
Author defends that verb form indicates time, answers objections, and refutes that the verb is compound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dialectical argument about verb form indicating time and identify the logical refinement (specific type of form) that resolves the objection.

Core Idea
The form indicates time, but the 'form' meant is the specific type, not the general pattern.

Arabic grammarians often use objections and answers to refine definitions. Here, the author defends that verb form (ṣīgha) indicates time, an objection based on active/passive forms is raised, and the answer introduces a crucial distinction: the 'specific type of form' (al-hay'a al-naw‘iyya). This lesson walks through that dialectical move, which is common in Islamic sciences like uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim
Find the author's thesis: the verb indicates time by its form (صيغة), not its material. The evidence is that the form changes when time changes (ضرب vs يضرب) and stays same when time is same despite different materials (ضرب and طلب).
The form is the morphological pattern, e.g., فَعَلَ for past, يَفْعَلُ for non-past.
2
Extract the Objection
The objector says: past active (ضَرَبَ) and past passive (ضُرِبَ) have different forms yet both indicate past time. So the evidence fails. Also, the imperfect (مضارع) has one form but indicates both present and future.
This is a clever counterexample: same time but different forms.
3
Analyze the Answer's Distinction
The author responds: the 'form' that varies with time is not just any form, but the 'specific type of form' (الهيئة النوعية). For each time, the language assigns multiple specific patterns (e.g., فَعَلَ, فَعِلَ for past; the passive pattern is one of them). Variation in these specific patterns does not break the general rule.
The distinction saves the thesis without denying the reality of different forms for same time.
4
Consider the Second Objection
A second objection: the imperfect verb must be compound because its prefixes (ت, أ, etc.) indicate the subject's state, while the rest indicates event and time – two separate parts. The author's answer begins but is cut off: 'the indicator is the verb by means of its prefixes, not...' implying the whole verb is a single unit.
This reinforces the earlier lesson that the verb is not compound; here the unity is defended against a morphological analysis.

والسكنات تدلُّ على الزَّمان ،المعيَّن، وذلك أنَّ الفعل يدلُّ على الحدث بمادته، وعلى الزمان بصيغته، والدَّليلُ على أنَّ الصِّيغة تدلُّ على الزَّمان اختلافه باختلافها، وإنِ اتَّحدت المادة كـ: «ضرب، يضرب، واتّحاد الزَّمان عند اتّحاد الهيئة، وإن اختلفت المادة ك: «ضرب، وطلب». واعترض هذا بأنَّ صيغة الماضي مِنَ المجهول مخالفة لصيغته من المعلوم، ولا اختلاف للزمان والمضارعُ يدلُّ على الحال والاستقبال ; على أنَّ الأصح ولا اختلاف لصيغته، فلا يتم الدليل . وأجيب : بأنَّ المراد بالهيئة التي يختلف الزَّمان باختلافها : الهيئة النَّوعيَّة; مثلاً : الواضعُ وضع للماضى صيغاً مختلفةً دلَّت هيئاتها المخصوصة المترادفة على الزَّمان الماضي، فمتى اختلفت تلك الهيئة المخصوصة اختلفت دلالة الصيغ. هي . فإن قيل : ينبغي أن يكون المضارع مركباً لا محالة; لدلالة زوائده على حال الفاعل، وباقيه على الحدث والزَّمان وهما جزئان ماديان. فالجواب : أنَّ الدَّالَّ الفعل بواسطة زوائده، لا
And the (vowel) rests indicate a specific time, and that is because the verb indicates the event by its material, and the time by its form. The evidence that the form indicates time is its variation with the variation of time, even if the material is the same, like 'ḍaraba' and 'yaḍribu', and the time is same when the structure is the same, even if the material differs, like 'ḍaraba' and 'ṭalaba'. And this was objected to: the form of the past passive differs from its form active, yet there is no difference in time, and the imperfect indicates present and future—while the correct view—and there is no difference in its form, so the evidence is not complete. And an answer was given: that what is meant by the structure that time varies with its variation is the specific type of structure; for example, the coiner put for the past various forms whose specific structures, though synonymous, indicate the past time—so whenever that specific structure differs, the indication of the forms differs. Then if it is said: the imperfect must be compound, because its prefixes indicate the state of the subject, and its remainder indicates the event and time, which are two material parts. The answer: that the indicator is the verb by means of its prefixes, not
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage, likely from the same commentary, discusses the indication of time by verb forms. The author asserts that the vowel-rests (sakanāt) i.e., the vowelling patterns, indicate a specific time. The verb indicates the event (ḥadath) by its material (letters) and time by its form (ṣīgha). The evidence: the form changes when the time changes, even if the material is the same (e.g., past ضرب vs. non-past يضرب), and the form remains the same when the time is the same, even if the material differs (e.g., ضرب and طلب, both past). An objection is raised: the form of the past passive (ضرب) differs from the past active (ضرب) but both indicate past time, so the evidence fails. Similarly, the imperfect (مضارع) indicates both present and future with the same form. The answer distinguishes between specific types of form (هيئة نوعية): the coiner assigned various specific forms for the past, each indicating the past time in a specific way. When the specific structure differs, the indication differs (e.g., active vs. passive have different specific forms, but both still indicate past). So the objection is answered. A further potential objection: the imperfect verb (مضارع) should be considered compound because it has prefixes (like ت, أ, etc.) that indicate the state of the subject, while the remainder indicates the event and time—two material parts. The answer begins: the indicator is the verb by means of its prefixes, not (that the parts are separate). The answer is incomplete but likely continues that the whole verb is a single unit, not a compound of separate parts. This lesson reinforces the earlier principle: the form (صيغة) is not a separate part; the verb is single, not compound, despite having morphemes.

صيغة
هيئة نوعية
مضارع

The specific type of form (الهيئة النوعية) is what varies with time, so active and passive past forms have different specific types but both indicate past.

Resolving Circularity: Concept vs Instance
Author resolves circularity objection via concept/instance distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve an objection of circularity by distinguishing between conceptual and existential dependence in definitions.

Core Idea
Circularity is avoided when dependence in concept is separated from dependence in instance.

An objector argues that defining mufrad in terms of murakkab and vice versa leads to circularity. The author shows that this objection confuses two distinct dimensions: conceptual dependence (mafhum) and existential dependence (masduq). Understanding this distinction is crucial for avoiding circular definitions in Islamic sciences, where precise conceptual relationships must be maintained without vicious cycles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector notes that a part precedes the whole, so mufrad (as a part) should be prior to murakkab (the whole). If mufrad depends on murakkab, then we have circularity: mufrad depends on murakkab and murakkab depends on mufrad. Find where this objection is stated in the Arabic.
The objection appears in the first lines: 'if the matter were reversed and the single depended on the compound as you mentioned, circularity would occur.'
2
Locate the key distinction
The author answers by saying 'the aspect is separate' (al-jiha munfakka). He distinguishes two aspects: concept (mafhum) and instance (masduq). Mufrad depends on murakkab in terms of concept, and murakkab depends on mufrad in terms of instance. Highlight these two pairs in the text.
The phrase 'من حيث المفهوم' and 'من حيث المصدوق' are used.
3
Apply the distinction to eliminate circularity
Because dependence is in different respects, there is no vicious circle: the conceptual priority of the compound over the single does not conflict with the existential priority of the single over the compound. Write out the two dependency relations separately.
This is a classic solution to apparent circularity in definitions.
4
Compare with analogous case: tasawwur and tasdiq
The author mentions that the same inquiry is raised regarding tasawwur and tasdiq. Although tasawwur is part of tasdiq (or a necessary concomitant), they are treated as co-extensive categories. The same distinction resolves that circularity. Read the analogy and note how the resolution transfers.
Al-Hilali is cited; this shows the broader applicability of the tool.
5
Consider the tanbih on restriction to conformity
The author notes a scholarly disagreement: should the divider (into mufrad and murakkab) be restricted to signification by conformity (dalil bi-l-mutabaqa)? Why does the author prefer the unrestricted definition? This step checks your understanding of how the definitional choice affects the argument.
This is a more advanced nuance; focus on the main logical move if pressed for time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (۲۸) وَهُوَ عَلَى قِسْمَيْنِ - أَعْنِي : المُفْرَدًا - :
قَصَارَةُ بَيَانُ الأوّل: أنَّ الجزء سابق على الكلِّ، فيكون الكل متوقفاً عليه فلو انعكس الأمر وكان المفرد متوقفاً على المركَّب كما ذكرتم ، لَزِمَ الدَّورُ .
وأمَّا الثَّاني: فظاهر.
وحاصلُ الجواب : أنَّ الجهة منفكَّةٌ، فإنَّ المفرد يتوقف على المركَّب من حيث المفهوم؛ أي : ما لا يدلُّ جزؤه، والمركَّب يتوقف عليه من حيث المصدوق، وكذلك تقابلهما من حيث المفهوم، وكون الجزء لازماً للكلِّ من حيث المصدوق.
وبالجملة كلِّ مِنَ المفرد والمركَّب له مفهوم و مصدوق، ومصدوق المركب ـ أي : ذاته ـ ؛ ك: «زيد قائم ونحوه متأخر عن مصدوق المفرد کـ: «زید» و«عمرو» ونحوهما، ومفهوم المركب ما دلّ جزؤه. .. إلخ متقدم على مفهوم المفرد، وهو ما لا يدلُّ جزوه؛ لأنَّ الثَّاني سلبٌ وهو للأوَّل، وسلبُ الشَّيء فرح عن وجوده ؛ قال الهلالي : وهذا البحث مثله يوردُ في تقسيم العلم إلى التَّصوُّر والتَّصديق؛ لأنَّ التَّصوُّر جزءٌ مِنَ التَّصديق عند الإمام، ولازم له عند غيره، ومع ذلك جعل قسيماً له، وجوابه ما ذكر آنفاً .
تنبيه :
لم يقيد المقسم إلى المفرد والمركَّب بـ «الدَّالّ بالمطابقة كما فعل الإمام الفخر والكاتبي، بل أطلق تبعاً لابن التلمساني في اعتراضه على الإمام، وهو ظاهر «الجُمَل»، و«المطالع»، و«المختصر » ؛ لأنَّ الوجوه التي وجّه بها التَّقييد بالمطابقة المذكورة حسبما في حواشي أبي حفص الفاسي على المختصر » إنَّما تدلُّ على أنَّه لا يصح تقييدُ الدَّالّ بالتَّضمن أوِ الالتزام، والمطلوب بيان العدول الإطلاق، أو التقييد بالمطابقة، ولا يدلُّ عليه شيءٌ مِنَ الوجوه. انظر: «حاشية أبي حفص، وبه تعلم أن قول شيخنا ابن منصور : الحقُّ التَّقييد كما فعل الفخر لا يتم.
His statement: (28) And it is of two types - I mean: the single word. The explanation of the first: the part precedes the whole, so the whole depends on it; if the matter were reversed and the single depended on the compound as you mentioned, circularity would occur. As for the second, it is obvious. The sum of the answer: the aspect is separate, for the single depends on the compound in terms of concept, i.e., what its part does not signify, and the compound depends on it in terms of instances, and likewise they are opposed in terms of concept, and the part is necessary for the whole in terms of instances. In summary, each of the single and compound has a concept and an instance. The instance of the compound, i.e., its essence, like 'Zayd is standing' and the like, is posterior to the instance of the single, like 'Zayd' and 'Amr' and the like. The concept of the compound, i.e., what its part signifies, etc., is prior to the concept of the single, i.e., what its part does not signify, because the second is a negation and is for the first, and the negation of a thing is a branch of its existence. Al-Hilali said: This same inquiry is raised in the division of knowledge into tasawwur and tasdiq, because tasawwur is a part of tasdiq according to the Imam, and a necessary concomitant according to others, yet it is made co-extensive with it. Its answer is what was mentioned previously. A caution: The divider into the single and compound did not restrict it to 'the signification by conformity' as did Imam Fakhr and al-Katibi, but rather left it unrestricted following Ibn al-Tilmasani in his objection to the Imam, and it is apparent in 'al-Jumal', 'al-Matali', and 'al-Mukhtasar'; because the aspects by which the restriction to the mentioned conformity was made, according to the marginal notes of Abu Hafs al-Fasi on 'al-Mukhtasar', only indicate that it is not correct to restrict the signification to involvement or commitment, and the required is to explain the divergence to being unrestricted or restricted to conformity, and nothing from these aspects indicates it. Look at: 'Hashiyat Abi Hafs, and by it you know that the statement of our teacher Ibn Mansur: 'The truth is the restriction as Fakhr did' is not complete.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion addressing an objection about the mutual dependence between a single term (mufrad) and a compound expression (murakkab). The author begins by quoting the poet's statement that the mufrad is divided into two types. An objector argues: since the part precedes the whole, the whole depends on the part; thus if the mufrad (part) were to depend on the murakkab (whole), circularity would result. The author responds by distinguishing two dimensions: concept (mafhum) and instance (masduq). The mufrad depends on the murakkab in terms of its concept (i.e., its definition as 'what does not signify a part' requires the concept of the compound for understanding), while the murakkab depends on the mufrad in terms of its instance (the actual existence of the compound presupposes the existence of its parts). Thus there is no circularity because the dependence is in different respects. The author then generalizes: every mufrad and murakkab has both a concept and an instance. The instance of the compound is posterior to the instance of its parts, while the concept of the compound (involving parts) is prior to the concept of the mufrad (which lacks parts). This is analogous to the relationship between tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent) in epistemology. The passage ends with a caution (tanbih) about whether the division should be restricted to signification by conformity (dalil bi-l-mutabaqa). The author notes that the commentator intentionally left it unrestricted, following Ibn al-Tilmasani's objection to restricting it, and argues that the justifications for restriction do not prove that it must be restricted to conformity over being unrestricted.

Key Terms
مفرد
مركب
مفهوم
مصدوق
دور

Checkpoint

A) By showing that mufrad is always prior to murakkab
B) By distinguishing between dependence in concept and dependence in instance
C) By denying that murakkab depends on mufrad
D) By redefining mufrad as a part of murakkab

Key Takeaway
Circularity in definition is resolved by distinguishing between conceptual dependence and existential dependence: the two can hold in opposite directions without conflict.

Universal and Particular Terms: Definitions and Examples
Author defines universal and particular as divisions of the single term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a universal term and a particular term based on the criterion of whether the concept prevents truth on multiple individuals, using examples from Arabic terms.

Core Idea
Universals share meaning across individuals; particulars do not – even if names coincide, the meaning is unique.

This lesson introduces the foundational division of single terms into universal and particular. The author explains that this division depends on whether the mere understanding of the term's meaning prevents its application to multiple individuals. You will learn to apply this criterion to distinguish terms like 'insan' (human) from 'Zayd'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division principle
Note that the single word (mufrad) is divided into universal and particular based on tashakhkhus (specificity) of meaning. If the meaning is specific to one individual, it is particular; if not, it is universal.
2
Extract the definition of universal
The universal is defined as 'that which is understood as admitting commonness in its meaning'. This means the mere conceptualization of its meaning does not prevent truth (sidq) on multiple individuals.
Example: 'insan' (human) applies to many persons.
3
Extract the definition of particular
The particular is defined as 'that which is not understood as having commonness in its meaning'. The conceptualization of its meaning prevents truth on multiple individuals.
Example: 'Zayd' refers to a specific person; even if many are named Zayd, it is due to multiple placements, not shared meaning.
4
Apply the criterion to examples
Test the definitions on given terms: 'asad' (lion) is universal in literal and metaphorical sense; 'Zayd' is particular. Note the author's clarification about homonyms.
The term 'aks (opposite) is used linguistically, not logically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في مباحث الألفاظ
أَفَادَ به أَنَّ المُفْرَدَ يَنْقَسِمُ بِحَسَبِ تَشَخُّصِ مَعْنَاهُ وَعَدَمِ تَشَخُّصِهِ إِلَى قِسْمَيْنِ : كُلِّيِّ، وَجُزْئِيِّ .
ثُمَّ أَشَارَ إِلَى تَعْرِيفِهِمَا بِقَوْلِهِ : (٢٩) فَمُفْهِمُ اشْتِرَاكِ «الكُلِّيُّ» كـ: «أَسَدٍ، وَعَكْسُهُ: «الجُزْنِيُّ» يَعْنِي : أَنَّ الكُلِّيَّ هُوَ : «الَّذِي يُفْهَمُ الشَّرِكَةُ فِي مَعْنَاهُ» ؛ أَيْ : لَا يَمْنَعُ نَفْسُ تَصُورِ مَعْنَاهُ مِنْ صِدْقِهِ عَلَى مُتَعَدِّدٍ كَـ : إِنْسَانِ وَ : «أَسَدٍ» ؛ فَإِنَّ كُلًّا مِنْهُمَا صَادِقٌ عَلَى أَفْرَادٍ، وَكَذَا «أَسَدٌ» كُلِّيِّ أَيْضاً بِاعْتِبَارِ مَعْنَاهُ المَجَازِيِّ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَصْدُقُ عَلَى : زَيْدٍ الشُّجَاعِ، وَعَمْرِو الشُّجَاعَ، وَغَيْرِهِمَا وَالجُزْئِيُّ هُوَ : «الَّذِي لَا يُفْهَمُ الشَّرِكَةُ فِي مَعْنَاهُ »بِأَنْ يَمْنَعَ نَفْسَ تَصُورِ مَعْنَاهُ مِنْ صِدْقِهِ عَلَى مُتَعَدِّدٍ كَـ : «زَيْدِ ، فَإِنَّ تَصُورَ الذَّاتِ المُعَيَّنَةِ المُسَمَّاةِ بِهِ يَمْنَعُ مِنْ صِدْقِهِ عَلَى مُتَعَدِّدٍ بِالضَّرُورَةِ، وَإِنْ شَارَكَ زَيْداً غَيْرَهُ فِي اسْمِهِ، فَلَيْسَ ذَلِكَ لِاشْتِرَاكِهِمَا فِي مَدْلُولٍ وَاحِدٍ، بَلْ لِتَعَدُّدِ الوَضْعِ، وَهَذَا مُرَادُهُ بِالعَكْسِ ؛ إِذِ العَكْسُ هُنَا بِالمَعْنَى اللُّغَوِيِّ أَيْضًا كَمَا مَرَّ .
Section on investigations of words. He indicated by it that the single word is divided according to the specificity of its meaning and its lack thereof into two types: universal and particular. Then he pointed to their definition by his statement: (29) So the universal is the one that implies commonness, like 'asad', and its opposite is the particular. He means: the universal is 'that which is understood [as admitting] commonness in its meaning'; i.e., the mere conceptualization of its meaning does not prevent its being true of multiple things, like 'insan' and 'asad'; for each of them is true of individuals. Likewise 'asad' is also universal by its metaphorical meaning, because it applies to Zayd the brave and 'Amr the brave and others. The particular is 'that which is not understood as having commonness in its meaning'; i.e., the mere conceptualization of its meaning prevents its being true of multiple things, like 'Zayd', for the conceptualization of the specific essence named by it prevents its being true of multiple necessarily, even if someone else shares the name, that is not due to their sharing a single referent, but due to multiple placements. And this is his meaning by 'opposite' (al-'aks), for 'aks here is also in the linguistic sense as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage is a definitional section that introduces the division of the single word (mufrad) into universal (kulli) and particular (juz'i). The author first states that this division is based on whether the meaning of the term is specific (tashakhkhus) or not. The universal is then defined: it is that which admits commonness (ishtirak) in its meaning, meaning that merely understanding the term's meaning does not prevent it from being true (sidq) of multiple individuals. Examples are given: 'insan' (human) applies to many persons, and 'asad' (lion) applies to many lions in its literal sense, and even metaphorically to brave men. The particular is defined as that which does not admit commonness: the very conceptualization of its meaning prevents it from being true of more than one individual, like the proper name 'Zayd'. The author clarifies that if two individuals share the name 'Zayd', it is not because the term refers to a common essence but because of multiple acts of naming (ta'addud al-wad'). The term 'aks (opposite) here is used in its linguistic sense, not as a logical conversion. This passage establishes the foundational distinction between universal and particular terms, which is essential for understanding predication and syllogistic reasoning in logic.

Key Terms
كلي
جزئي
تصور
صدق
وضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is 'Zayd' a particular term even though many people are named Zayd?
A) Because the term 'Zayd' can be true of many individuals
B) Because each 'Zayd' shares a common essence
C) Because the conceptualization of the named essence prevents truth on multiple, and name-sharing is due to multiple placements
D) Because 'Zayd' is a universal in the metaphorical sense

Key Takeaway
A universal term admits commonness in its meaning and applies to many, while a particular term has a meaning that is specific to one individual, even if the same word is used for others by different acts of naming.

Understanding Haml al-Muwata'ah and the Basis of Universal Division
Author defines haml al-muwata'ah and clarifies universal/particular division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify haml al-muwata'ah, distinguish it from predications of derivation and relation, and explain why the universal/particular division primarily applies to meanings, not words.

Core Idea
Co-extensive predication is the foundation of definition; universal/particular are properties of meanings, not words.

In Islamic logic, not all predications are alike. Some predicates apply to their subjects directly and co-extensively, while others require a derivation or relation. This lesson unpacks the first type—haml al-muwata'ah—and reveals a critical subtlety: when logicians call a term 'universal' or 'particular', they are really referring to the concept (ma'na), not the word itself. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion in later logical operations.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of haml al-muwata'ah
Find the explicit definition in the Arabic: 'affirming a thing of another without an intermediary of derivation or relation.' This is the core of co-extensive predication.
2
Contrast with other types
Note the examples: 'Zayd is a human' is co-extensive; 'Zayd is knowledgeable' is derivational; 'Amr is possessor of knowledge' is relational.
Derivation (ishtiqaq) uses a derived form; relation (idafa) uses a possessive construction.
3
Identify the key example of 'knowledge'
The author shows that 'knowledge' is not universal when predicated of individuals (Zayd, 'Amr) because it does not apply by co-extension, but it is universal when predicated of disciplines (logic, grammar) because it applies by co-extension.
This illustrates that universality depends on the mode of predication, not the word itself.
4
Understand the metaphor
The division into universal/particular is primarily for meanings (ma'na). Words are called universal only metaphorically, because the signifier takes the name of the signified.
This is an important principle in Islamic logic: don't confuse linguistic categories with conceptual ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حَمْلُ المُوَاطَأَةِ دُونَ حَمْلِ الاشْتِفَاقِ وَالإِضَافَةِ، وَهُوَ - أَيْ : حَمْلُ المُوَاطَأَةِ -: «إِثْبَاتُ شَيْءٍ لآخَرَ بِلَا وَاسِطَةِ اشْتِقَاقٍ أَوْ إِضَافَةٍ؛ كَـ : إِثْبَاتِ الإِنْسَانِ لِزَيْدٍ وَعَمْرِو فِي قَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ إِنْسَانٌ، وَعَمْرُو إِنْسَانُ، بِخِلَافِ إِثْبَاتِ العِلْمِ لَهُمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ عَالِمٌ، وَعَمْرُو ذُو عِلْمٍ، فَالعِلْمُ لَا يَكُونُ كُلِّيَّا بِاعْتِبَارِ حَمْلِهِ عَلَى زَيْدٍ وَعَمْرِو ؛ إِذْ لَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِمَا مُوَاطَأَةً، وَإِنَّمَا يَكُونُ كُلِّيَّا بِاعْتِبَارِ حَمْلِهِ عَلَى المَنْطِقِ وَالنَّحْوِ مَثَلاً لِصِحَّةِ قَوْلِكَ : «المَنْطِقُ عِلْمٌ، وَالنَّحْو عِلْمٌ». وَهَذَا التَّقْسِيمُ بِحَسَبِ الحَقِيقَةِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ لِلْمَعْنَى؛ إِذْ هُوَ مَعْرُوضُ التَّشَخْصِ وَعَدَمِهِ، وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَ اللَّفْظُ : «كُلِّيَّا» وَ: «جُزْئيًا) مَجَازاً مُرْسَلاً؛ مِنْ تَسْمِيَةِ الدَّالّ بِاسْمِ مَدْلُولِهِ .
Predication of co-extension (haml al-muwata'ah) without predication of derivation or relation; it is - i.e., predication of co-extension -: affirming a thing of another without an intermediary of derivation or relation; like: affirming 'human' of Zayd and 'Amr in your saying: Zayd is a human, and 'Amr is a human, contrary to affirming 'knowledge' of them in your saying: Zayd is knowledgeable, and 'Amr is possessor of knowledge; for knowledge is not universal by virtue of its predication on Zayd and 'Amr, since it does not apply to them by co-extension; rather, it becomes universal by virtue of its predication on logic and grammar, for example, because of the correctness of your saying: Logic is a knowledge, and grammar is a knowledge. And this division, according to reality, is only for the meaning, for it is the subject of individuation and its absence; the word is only called 'universal' and 'particular' metaphorically, from naming the signifier with the name of its significate.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage defines 'haml al-muwata'ah' (predication of co-extension) and distinguishes it from predication of derivation and relation. The author explains that in co-extension, the predicate applies to the subject without any mediating factor, as in 'Zayd is a human' and 'Amr is a human'. In contrast, 'knowledge' is not predicated of Zayd and 'Amr by co-extension because it is not true of them in the same sense without derivation; rather, it is predicated of 'logic' and 'grammar' by co-extension. The author then clarifies that the division of terms into universal and particular is essentially for meanings, because meanings are what are subject to individuation or lack thereof. Words are called universal or only metaphorically, by naming the signifier with the name of the significate. This is a key point: when logicians speak of 'universal' and 'particular', they primarily refer to concepts, not the words that express them. The example of 'knowledge' is instructive: 'knowledge' is a universal when applied to disciplines like logic and grammar, but not when applied to individual persons like Zayd and 'Amr, because it is not truly predicated of them in the same way without a derivative form. This distinction is foundational for understanding how definitions work in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
حمل المواطأة
كلي
جزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of haml al-muwata'ah?
A) Zayd is knowledgeable ('alim)
B) Zayd is a human (insan)
C) Zayd is possessor of knowledge (dhu 'ilm)
D) Logic is a knowledge ('ilm)

Key Takeaway
Haml al-muwata'ah is predication without derivation; universal/particular divisions apply to meanings, not words.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify whether the following predications are haml al-muwata'ah or not: (a) 'al-Khalq is qadim' (b) 'al-'Alim is 'alim' (c) 'Zayd is a faqih' (where faqih means knowledgeable in fiqh). Explain your reasoning.
?

Refining the Definition of Particular
Author explains why 'nafs tasawwurihi' is added to the definition of particular and lists three types of universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish whether a concept is particular or universal by identifying whether the prevention of multiple applicability is internal (from the conception itself) or external.

Core Idea
A concept is particular only if its own conception prevents multiplicity, not if external reality does.

In this passage, the author fine-tunes the definition of 'particular' by explicitly requiring that the prevention of predicating it to many must arise from the mere conception of the concept. This subtle but crucial condition ensures that concepts like 'partner to God' or 'sea of mercury'—which have no actual instances—are correctly classified as universal, not particular. Understanding this distinction is essential for precise logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the key addition
Find the phrase 'nafs tasawwurihi' (the conception itself) in the Arabic. Why did the author add this? What problem does it solve?
The addition prevents misclassifying concepts that are externally impossible as particular.
2
Contrast internal vs external prevention
Identify in the text where the author says prevention can be from 'outside' (kharij). Understand that a concept is particular only if the prevention is from the concept itself (dhati), not from external impossibility.
3
Apply to given examples
Take the examples: 'partner to God' (sharik) and 'Zayd'. Check each: Does the concept itself prevent multiple instances? For 'partner', no; for 'Zayd', yes. Hence 'partner' is universal despite no existence.
This shows the refined definition in action.
4
Recognize the three types announced
The author states there are three types of universals with no existing individuals. The passage describes the first type in detail. Note that the enumeration continues; you have learned the first type.
The passage is cut off, but the first type covers both impossible and possible non-existing concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالمُرَادُ بِـ تَصَوُّرِ المَعْنَى: حُصُولُهُ فِي الذَّهْنِ، لَا حُصُولَ صُورَتِهِ ; إِذْ هُوَ نَفْسُ الصُّورَةِ وَإِلَّا لَزِمَ إِثْبَاتُ الصُّورَةِ لِلصُّورَةِ. وَقُلْنَا : نَفْسُ تَصَوُّرِهِ ; لِلتَّنْبِيهِ عَلَى أَنَّ المُعْتَبَرَ فِي كَوْنِهِ جُزْنِيًّا أَنْ يَكُونَ المَنْعُ مِنْ صِدْقِهِ عَلَى مُتَعَدِّدٍ نَاشِئَاً مِنْ مُجَرَّدِ تَصَوُّرِهِ، وَأَنَّهُ لَا عِبْرَةً بِمَنْعِ الصِّدْقِ عَلَى مُتَعَدِّدٍ إِذَا كَانَ المَنْعُ مَأخُوذاً مِنْ خَارِج فَقَط ، فَلَا يُسَمَّى بِسَبَبِهِ جُزْيْيًّا، بَلْ هُوَ كُلِّيِّ، فَدَخَلَ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الكُلِّيِّ ثَلَاثَةُ أَنْوَاعِ : أَحَدُهَا : مَا لَمْ يُوجَدْ مِنْ أَفْرَادِهِ شَيْءٌ؛ سَوَاءٌ : اسْتَحَالَ وُجُودُهُ كَـ : الشَّرِيكِ، وَالجَمْعِ بَيْنَ الضَّدَّيْنِ؛ فَإِنَّ كُلَّا مِنْهُمَا لَا يَمْنَعُ نَفْسُ تَصَوُّرِهِ مِنْ صِدْقِهِ عَلَى مُتَعَدِّدٍ ; إِلَّا أَنَّ وُجُودَ شَيْءٍ مِنْ ذَلِكَ فِي الخَارِجِ مُسْتَحِيلٌ. أَمْ لَمْ يَسْتَحِلْ كَـ: بَحْرٍ مِنْ زِئْبَقٍ، وَجَبَلٍ مِنْ يَاقُوتٍ; فَإِنَّ العَقْلَ يُجَوِّزُ صِدْقَ
And what is meant by 'conception of the meaning' is its obtaining in the mind, not the obtaining of its form, because it is the form itself; otherwise, affirming the form for the form would be necessary. And we said: 'the conception itself' to draw attention to the fact that what is considered in its being particular is that the prevention from its applicability to many arises from its mere conception, and that no attention is paid to the prevention of applicability to many if the prevention is taken only from outside; thus it is not called particular because of it, rather it is universal. So there enter into the definition of the universal three types: The first: what no individual of it exists, whether: its existence is impossible, like the partner (to God) and the combination of opposites; for each of them, its mere conception does not prevent its applicability to many; except that the existence of any of that in the external is impossible. Or it is not impossible, like a sea of mercury and a mountain of ruby; for the intellect permits its applicability to ...
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage refines the definition of 'particular' (juz'i) by adding the condition 'nafs tasawwurihi' (the conception itself). The author explains that a concept is particular only when the prevention of its being predicated of many arises from the concept itself, not from an external factor. If the prevention is external—such as the impossibility of there being more than one God, or the combination of opposites—the concept remains universal. The author then lists three types of concepts that are universal but have no actual individuals: (1) those whose existence is impossible, like a partner to God (sharik) or the combination of opposites, (2) those whose existence is possible but not actualized, like a sea of mercury or a mountain of ruby, and (3) a third type not fully described in this passage. The key insight is that the definition of particular depends on the internal conceptual content, not on external reality. For example, the concept of 'partner to God' is universal because the mind can conceive of multiple partners, even though such existence is impossible. In contrast, the concept of 'Zayd' is particular because the mind cannot conceive of it applying to many without contradiction. An Islamic example: 'the combination of truth and falsehood in one statement' is impossible, yet the concept itself does not prevent multiplicity, so it is universal.

Key Terms
تصور المعنى
نفس التصور
الجزئي

Key Takeaway
The refined definition of particular requires that the prevention of multiple applicability must come from the conception itself, not from external factors like actual impossibility—thus many non-existing concepts remain universal.

Only the Noun Divides into Universal and Particular
States that only the noun divides into universal and particular; the verb is always universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which part of speech can be universal or particular and explain why the verb is always universal.

Core Idea
The verb’s event applies to many subjects, making it always universal.

In classical logic and grammar, words are categorized into noun, verb, and particle. Only the noun can be universal or particular; the verb is always universal. This distinction is crucial for understanding predication in Islamic sciences, as it affects how we analyze statements about actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parts of speech
The passage lists three divisions of the single word: noun (ism), verb (fi‘l), and particle (ḥarf). Note that these are the basic categories.
2
Recognize which divides into universal/particular
The text explicitly states that only the noun is divided into universal and particular. The verb and particle are not.
This is the key claim.
3
Understand why the verb is always universal: the event
Consider the event (ḥadath) that the verb expresses. Even if the event occurs at a specific time, it can be applied to many subjects (e.g., 'hit' can be done by many). Therefore, the verb is universal.
The event does not prevent truth on many.
4
Consider the attribution (isnād)
The attribution of the verb to a specific subject is particular, but the event itself is universal. The commentary clarifies that the correctness of predicating the verb to many is due to the event, not the attribution.
Thus overall the verb remains universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تَنْبِيهَات:
(۱) - الأَوَّلُ : المُفْرَدُ ثَلَاثَةُ أَقْسَام : اسْمٌ، وَفِعْلٌ، وَحَرْفٌ. وَالمُقْسَمُ مِنْهَا إِلَى : كُلِّيٌّ وَجُزْنِي إِنَّمَا هُوَ الاِسْمُ .
وَأَمَّا الفِعْلُ فَهُوَ كُلِّيٌّ دَائِماً ؛ سَوَاءٌ اعْتَبَرْنَا الحَدَثَ ، أَوِ الإِسْنَادَ؛ لِصِحَّةِ حَمْلِهِ
قوله : (اسْمٌ، وَفِعْلٌ، وَحَرْفٌ) وبعضهم يسمِّي «الفعل» : كلمة، و«الحرف»: أداةً. قوله: (سَوَاءٌ اعْتَبَرْنَا الحَدَثَ) أي : الواقع في أحد الأزمنة، وهو لا يمنع من صدقه على كثير ، ولذلك صح اتصافُ كلِّ مِنَ الفاعلين بذلك الحدث. قوله : (أَوِ الإِسْنَادَ) أي : النِّسبة . الصَّواب : أنَّه جزئي باعتبار النسبة، وصحة حمله على كثيرين باعتبار الحدث لا باعتبار النسبة كما هو ظاهره.
Notifications: (1) The first: the single word is three divisions: noun, verb, and particle. And that which is divided into universal and particular among them is only the noun. And as for the verb, it is always universal; whether we consider the event, or the attribution; due to the correctness of predicating it. He said: ('noun, verb, and particle') and some call the 'verb' a word, and the 'particle' a tool. He said: ('whether we consider the event') meaning: the one occurring in one of the times, and it does not prevent its truth on many, and therefore every one of the doers is described by that event. He said: ('or the attribution') meaning: the relation. The correct view: that it is particular with respect to the attribution, and the correctness of predicating it to many is with respect to the event not with respect to the attribution as is apparent.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage presents the first notification: the single word (mufrad) is divided into three types: noun (ism), verb (fi'l), and particle (harf). Among these, only the noun is divided into universal and particular. The verb is always universal, whether one considers the event (hadath) or the attribution (isnad). That is because an event can be applied to many subjects, so the verb is predicable of many. The commentary adds that some call the verb 'kalima' and the particle 'adatan'. It notes that considering the event, it occurs in a particular time but still applies to many; considering the attribution, it is actually particular because the attribution is to a specific subject, but the event aspect ensures universality.

Key Terms
مُفْرَد (mufrad): Single word, as opposed to compound expression; divided into ism, fi'l, harf.
اسْم (ism): Noun; the only part of speech that can be divided into universal and particular.
فِعْل (fi‘l): Verb; always universal because the event it expresses applies to many.
حَرْف (ḥarf): Particle; a type of word not divided into universal/particular.
حَدَث (ḥadath): Event; the verb conveys an event that can be predicated of many.
إِسْنَاد (isnād): Attribution; the relation of the verb to its subject, which is particular but the event is universal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Verb
C) Particle
D) Both noun and verb

Key Takeaway
Only the noun can be universal or particular; the verb is always universal due to its event being applicable to many.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Critique of a Logical Example: Na'im al-Janna
Author critiques an example and suggests a better one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the appropriateness of a logical example by distinguishing actual from potential infinity in Islamic contexts.

Core Idea
The best logical examples unambiguously instantiate the intended property without contextual confusion.

This passage from the hashiya tradition shows a commentator refining a logical example. Some scholars used 'the bliss of Paradise' to illustrate an infinite set, but the commentator objects because the actual existing instances are finite. He suggests using 'the perfection of Allah' instead. This teaches us that choosing the right example requires precision and awareness of the distinction between actual and potential infinity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the property to be illustrated
Determine what logical property the example is supposed to demonstrate. Here, it is an infinite set or unlimited individuals.
2
Examine the proposed example
Check whether 'na'im al-janna' truly has that property. Only future bliss is infinite; past bliss that has occurred is finite.
3
Assess the objection
Why does the author reject the example? Because the discussion concerns existing individuals, and all actual instances of bliss are finite. The example conflates actual finitude with potential future infinity.
4
Evaluate the alternative
Why does 'kamal al-Bari' work better? God's perfection is eternal, and no proof shows that infinitude in the eternal is impossible. Thus it avoids the confusion between actual and potential infinity.
5
Generalize the criterion
What makes an example appropriate in logic? It must unambiguously possess the relevant attribute without contextual limitations or temporal restrictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَثَّلَ لَهُ بَعْضُ العُلَمَاءِ بـ: «نَعِيمِ الجَنَّةِ»؛ وَرُدَّ : بِأَنَّ الكَلَامَ فِيمَا وُجِدَ مِنَ الْأَفْرَادِ، وَمَا دَخَلَ الوُجُودَ مِنْ أَفْرَادِ النَّعِيمِ فَهُوَ مُتَنَاهِ، وَإِنَّمَا يُوْصَفُ النَّعِيمُ بِعَدَمِ التَّنَاهِي بِاعْتِبَارِ المُسْتَقْبَلِ . وَلَوْ مُثْلَ لَهُ بِـ: كَمَالِ البَارِي تَعَالَى»؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَمْ يَقُمْ دَلِيلٌ عَلَى اسْتِحَالَةِ عَدَم النِّهَايَةِ فِي القَدِيمِ كَمَا قَالَهُ المَنْجُورُ وَغَيْرُهُ، لَكَانَ أَقْرَبَ .
And some scholars gave an example of it with 'the bliss of Paradise'; and it was rejected because the discussion concerns what exists of the individuals, and what has entered existence from the individuals of bliss is finite, and bliss is described as infinite only with respect to the future. And if it were illustrated with 'the perfection of Allah the Exalted' because no proof has been established for the impossibility of infinitude in the eternal, as al-Manjur and others said, it would be more appropriate.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of an example used in logic. The author is discussing an example for something (likely an infinite series or a universal with infinite instances). Some scholars used 'the bliss of Paradise' as an example. The author rejects this because the discussion focuses on existing individuals; all actual instances of bliss that have come into existence are finite. The bliss is described as infinite only in the future sense. As a better example, the author suggests 'the perfection of Allah the Exalted' because no proof rules out infinite attributes in the eternal, as al-Manjur and others stated. Thus this example is more appropriate. This is a typical hashiya correction, arguing that the original example is flawed due to a confusion between actual and potential infinity.

Key Terms
نَعِيمِ الجَنَّةِ
كَمَالِ البَارِي

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the author prefers a different logical example.

Key Takeaway
A good logical example must unambiguously instantiate the intended property without being limited by temporal or contextual factors.

Classification of Mufrad: Noun, Verb, Particle, and Their Universality
Author explains that only nouns are divided into universal/particular; verbs are always universal; particles are particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any single word (mufrad) as a noun, verb, or particle and determine whether it is universal or particular based on the criteria given in this passage.

Core Idea
Only nouns are divided into universal and particular; verbs are always universal, particles are always particular.

This passage from al-Bannani's commentary clarifies a crucial point about the single word (al-mufrad): not all words are divided into universal and particular. Understanding which categories are universal and which are particular is essential for applying logical definitions correctly in Islamic sciences. Let's examine the reasoning behind each category.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions of mufrad
The passage states that al-mufrad is divided into ism, fi'l, and harf. Note these three categories and their definitions.
These are the standard parts of speech in Arabic grammar.
2
Analyze the noun's status
Only the noun is divided into universal and particular. This means nouns can refer to a general category (e.g., 'man') or a specific individual (e.g., 'Zayd'). Understand that this division applies only to nouns.
Nouns can be either universal or particular depending on their meaning.
3
Understand why verbs are always universal
The author explains that even if a verb refers to a specific event, its meaning can be predicated of many agents. For example, 'stood' can apply to many people. Therefore, verbs are always universal.
Consider the verb 'wajaba' (it is obligatory) - it applies to many actions.
4
Resolve the particle's status
Some claim particles are neither universal nor particular, but the correct view is that they are particular because their meaning depends on their object of entry (e.g., 'min' means 'from' in a specific context).
The passage cites al-Adud and al-Sayyid for this view.
5
Apply the classification
Given any word from an Islamic text, determine its part of speech and universality. Example: 'al-kitāb' (book) is a noun and can be universal or particular; 'kataba' (he wrote) is a verb and is universal; 'fī' (in) is a particle and is particular.
Practice with words from the Qur'an or hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تَنْبِيهَات: (۱) - الأَوَّلُ : المُفْرَدُ ثَلَاثَةُ أَقْسَام : اسْمٌ، وَفِعْلٌ، وَحَرْفٌ. وَالمُقْسَمُ مِنْهَا إِلَى : كُلِّيٌّ وَجُزْنِي إِنَّمَا هُوَ الاِسْمُ . وَأَمَّا الفِعْلُ فَهُوَ كُلِّيٌّ دَائِماً؛ سَوَاءٌ اعْتَبَرْنَا الحَدَثَ، أَوِ الإِسْنَادَ؛ لِصِحَّةِ حَمْلِهِ عَلَى كَثِيرٍ مِنَ الفَاعِلِينَ. وَأَمَّا الحَرْفُ : فَقِيلَ : إِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِكُلِّي وَلَا جُزْنِي لأَنَّهُ لَا مَعْنَى لَهُ فِي نَفْسِهِ، وَإِنَّمَا مَعْنَاهُ فِي مَدْخُولِهِ . وَاعْتُرِضَ : بِأَنَّ الحَرْفَ مُفْرَدٌ قطعاً، وَالكُلِّيَّةُ والجُزْنِيَّةُ بِاعْتِبَارِ المُفْرَدِ عَلَى طَرَفَيْ نَقِيضِ لَا يُمْكِنُ رَفْعَهُمَا . وَالحَقُّ : أَنَّ مَعْنَى الحَرْفِ جُزْنِيٌّ ; لِمَا فِي رِسَالَةِ العَضُدِ، وَأَوْضَحَهُ السَّيِّدُ فِي بَحْثِ
Warnings: (1) - The first: The single word has three divisions: noun, verb, and particle. And that which is divided into universal and particular among them is only the noun. As for the verb, it is always universal, whether we consider the event or the attribution, because of the correctness of its predication to many agents. As for the particle, it is said: It is neither universal nor particular because it has no meaning in itself, but its meaning is in its object of entry. And it was objected that the particle is definitely a single word, and universality and particularity, with respect to the single word, are on two sides of a contradiction that cannot be removed. And the truth is that the meaning of the particle is particular, as in the Epistle of al-Adud, and al-Sayyid clarified it in his discussion.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion on the division of al-mufrad (the single word) into noun, verb, and particle, and their status regarding universality and particularity. The author (Bannani) states a warning: the single word is divided into three types. Only the noun is divided into universal and particular. The verb is always universal, whether we consider its event (al-hadath) or its attribution (al-isnad), because it can be predicated of many agents. This is explained by the fact that verbs like 'stood' can apply to many subjects. As for the particle, there is disagreement: some say it is neither universal nor particular because it has no independent meaning—its meaning is in what it enters (e.g., prepositions). But an objection is raised: the particle is definitely a single word, and universality and particularity are two contradictory attributes; every single word must be one or the other. The correct view is that the meaning of the particle is particular, as established in al-Adud's epistle and clarified by al-Sayyid. This means that particles refer to specific relationships or meanings.

Key Terms
المُفْرَد (al-mufrad): The single word; three types: noun, verb, particle.
الاِسْمُ (al-ism): Noun; the only part of speech divided into universal/particular.
الفِعْل (al-fi'l): Verb; always universal due to predication to many agents.
الحَرْف (al-harf): Particle; correct view is it is particular, not lacking universality/particularity.
الکلیه و الجزئية (al-kulliyya wa al-juz'iyya): Universality and particularity; contradictory attributes applicable to single words.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the status of the particle (harf) regarding universality and particularity?
A) It is always universal.
B) It is neither universal nor particular.
C) It is particular.
D) It is sometimes universal and sometimes particular.

Key Takeaway
The essential logical point: universality and particularity apply only to nouns; verbs are always universal, particles are always particular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following words from an Islamic text: الصلاة (prayer), يصلي (he prays), في (in). For each, determine if it is a noun, verb, or particle, and whether it is universal or particular. Explain your reasoning based on the passage.
?
Consider the verb 'يكتب' (he writes). Why is it always universal even if it refers to a specific act of writing by Zayd? Provide a reason using the criteria from the passage.

The Universality of Particles and Verbs: Two Positions
Author presents two views on whether particles and verbs are universal or particular in meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the muhaqqiqun's and nahwiyyun's positions on the wad' of particles and verbs, and summarize the key arguments for each.

Core Idea
Particles and verbs are either universal in placement or particular — a foundational debate in usul al-fiqh and mantiq.

In classical Islamic logic and linguistics, a key debate concerns whether particles and verbs are placed as universals or particulars. The verifying scholars (muhaqqiqun) and grammarians (nahwiyyun) hold opposing views. This lesson unpacks the two positions and the reasoning behind them, using examples like 'ana' (I) to illustrate the implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two camps
Find the two groups mentioned: 'أكثر المحققين' (muhaqqiqun) and 'أكثر النحويين' (nahwiyyun). Note that the author contrasts their views.
2
Extract each claim
Muhaqqiqun say particles/verbs are universal in placement, particular in usage. Nahwiyyun say they are placed for particulars. Write down the exact phrases.
For muhaqqiqun: 'وضعت كلية، وإنما عرضت لها الجزئية عند الاستعمال'. For nahwiyyun: 'وضعت لجزئي'.
3
Analyze the example of 'ana'
The muhaqqiqun argue that 'ana' is placed for the universal concept of 'speaker', not for one specific person. If it were for one person, it would be metaphorical for others; if for all, it would require infinite homonyms. So it must be universal in wad' and becomes specified by context.
4
Understand the nahwiyyun's objection
Nahwiyyun argue that if particles were universal in wad', they would never be used for that universal meaning in actual speech, making them metaphors without literal realities. This is problematic because language scholars debated whether metaphor implies a literal meaning.
The example of 'al-Rahman' is given as a rare case where a word might lack a literal meaning.
5
Grasp the nuance of 'particularity'
The author clarifies that the particularity of a particle is not grammatical but conceptual: when you conceive its meaning, you conceive it as a particular instance, not that you verbally predicate particularity of it.
This avoids the objection that particles cannot be described as particular in a sentence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في مباحث الألفاظ
وَأَكْثَرُ المُحَقِّقِينَ عَلَى الأَوَّلِ ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّهَا وُضِعَتْ كُلِّيَّةٌ، وَإِنَّمَا عُرِضَتْ لَهَا الجُزْيَّةُ عِنْدَ الِاسْتِعْمَالِ ، فَهِيَ كُلِّيَّةٌ وَضْعاً جُزْئِيَّةٌ اسْتِعْمَالاً .
وَأَكْثَرُ النَّحَوِيِّينَ عَلَى الثَّانِي ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّهَا وُضِعَتْ لِجُزْنِيِّ ؛
قصارة.
نعم؛ الحكم باعتبار الحدث؛ لأنَّه مأخوذ في مفهومه، فلذا كان الفعل مسنداً دائماً، فامتاز عن الحرف باعتبار اشتمال معناه على ما هو مسند إلى غيره، بخلاف الحرف؛ إذ ليس له معنى يصلح لأن يكون مسنداً . اهـ وما بينه في وضع الحرف والفعل مسلَّمٌ لكن لا ينتج له مدعاه؛ إذ لا شكَّ أنَّ الفرد الواحد المعيَّن مِنَ الابتداء يمنع تصوره ... إلخ، وأيضاً قوله : «لا يصح أن يحكم عليه يقال عليه : ليس معنى اتصاف الحرف بالجزئية أنَّك تحمل عليه بالجزئية وتصفه لفظاً بها حتّى يلزم ما ذكرت، بل معنى الجزئيّة فيه : أنَّك إذا تعقّلت معناه تتعقله جزئيًّا، وحاصله : أنَّ غاية ما هنا انصاف في نفس الأمر دون وصف وحكم عليه بذلك.
قوله : (وَأَكْثَرُ المُحَقِّقِينَ) منهم : أبو حيان، والرّضي، والسعد، والقرافي.
قوله : (بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّهَا وُضِعَتْ كُلِّيَّةٌ) أي : فـ «أنا» مثلاً موضوع لمفهوم المتكلم، فإذا استعمله متكلّم معيَّن تعيَّن بواسطة قرينة التّكلُّم ، وهذا مثلاً موضوع لمشار إليه مفرد مذكّر فإذا أشير به له تعيَّن بواسطة الإشارة الحسية ، . . وهكذا .
وليست لفظة «أنا موضوعةً لواحد مِنَ الأشخاص بعينه وإلا كانت في غيره مجازاً، ولا لكلِّ واحدٍ منها وإلا كانت مشتركة موضوعة أوضاعاً بعدد أفراد المتكلّم وذلك مستبعد جدا، فوجب أن تكون موضوعة لمفهوم كلّيّ شامل لكل الأفراد، ويكون الغرض من وضعها له استعمالها في أفراده المعيَّنة دونه، فتكون معيَّنةً لا جزئيَّةٌ.
قوله : (وَأَكْثَرُ النَّحَوِيِّينَ) هذا هو التحقيق، وهو الذي اعتمده العضد والسَّيِّد في «حواشي المطوّل»، والدَّماميني في شرح التسهيل»، فلا يلزم الاشتراك ولا المجاز، ولو كانت كما قال الأول كانت مجازات لا حقائق لها ؛ إذ لم تستعمل فيما وضعت له من ذلك المفهوم الكلّي يوماً ما ولو كان الأمر كذلك لما اختلفت أئمَّة اللُّغة في عدم استلزام المجاز الحقيقة، وإنما احتج من نفى الاستلزام إلى التَّمسُّك بأمثلة نادرة :: الرَّحمن ؛ لكون ذلك منتشراً جدًّا
Chapter on Topics of Words. Most of the verifying scholars (muhaqqiqun) are upon the first [view] — based on the fact that they [particles and verbs] were placed as universals, and particularity only occurs to them during usage, so they are universal in placement and particular in usage. Most of the grammarians (nahwiyyun) are upon the second [view] — based on the fact that they were placed for particulars. [Marginal note: Qisara?] Yes; the judgment is by consideration of the event, because it is taken into its concept, therefore the verb is always attributed, and it is distinguished from the particle by virtue of its meaning containing something attributed to something else, unlike the particle, for it has no meaning that is valid to be attributed. End. And what he clarified in the placement of the particle and verb is granted, but it does not produce his claim; since there is no doubt that the singular individual specified from the beginning prevents its conception... etc. Also his statement: 'It is not valid to judge it, it is said: The meaning of the particle's particularity is not that you predicate particularity of it and describe it verbally thereby so that what you mentioned is necessary, rather the meaning of particularity in it is that when you conceive its meaning you conceive it as particular. The gist is that all here is a qualification in the thing itself without description and judgment thereby.' His statement: 'And most of the muhaqqiqun' — among them: Abu Hayyan, al-Radi, al-Sa'd, and al-Qarafi. His statement: 'Based on the fact that they were placed as universals' — i.e., for example, 'ana' is placed for the concept of the speaker. When a specific speaker uses it, it becomes specified by the context of speech. And 'hadha' for example is placed for a pointed-to singular masculine; when one points with it to him, it becomes specified by sensory pointing, and so on. And the word 'ana' is not placed for one particular person, otherwise it would be metaphorical in others, nor for each one of them, otherwise it would be homonymous placed with placements equal to the number of speaker individuals, which is very unlikely. Therefore it must be placed for a universal concept inclusive of all individuals, and the purpose of its placement for it is to use it in its specified individuals without it, so it becomes specified but not particular. His statement: 'And most of the grammarians' — this is the verified position, and it is the one relied upon by al-Aḍud and al-Sayyid in 'Hawashi al-Mutawwal' and al-Damamini in 'Sharh al-Tashil'. Thus neither homonymy nor metaphor is necessary. And if they were as the first [group] says, they would be metaphors without realities; since they were never used in that universal concept for which they were placed even once. If that were the case, the imams of language would not have disagreed about the non-necessity of metaphor for reality. Indeed, the one who denied the necessity only resorted to rare examples like 'al-Rahman', because that [absence of reality] is very widespread.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn (likely al-Sullam) that discusses whether particles and verbs are universal or particular in their meaning. The author begins by stating two positions: the majority of verifiers (muhaqqiqun) hold that these words were placed as universals but become particular in usage; the majority of grammarians (nahwiyyun) hold that they were placed for particulars. A marginal note 'قصارة' (possibly Qisara) appears. The author then clarifies that the particularity of a particle is not in terms of grammatical judgment but in mental conception: when you conceive its meaning, you conceive it as a particular, not that you predicate particularity of it verbally. He then elaborates on the muhaqqiqun's position using the example of 'ana' (I). The word 'ana' is placed for the universal concept of 'speaker', but when a specific person uses it, it becomes specified. If it were placed for a specific person, it would be metaphorical for others; if for all speakers, it would require infinite homonyms. Thus it must be universal in placement. He then supports the grammarians' position, citing authorities like al-Aḍud and al-Sayyid, and argues that if the muhaqqiqun's position were true, these words would be pure metaphors without literal meanings, which is problematic because language scholars would not have debated the necessity of metaphor implying a literal meaning. The example of 'al-Rahman' (a name of Allah) is given as a rare case where a word might lack a literal meaning. The passage ends with the observation that such cases are very widespread if the muhaqqiqun's view is taken.
