I
And similarly, there is also a disagreement regarding the names of sciences, such as 'al-mantiq' and 'al-nahw' for example. And the truth: that if what is meant by them is the specific rules, then they are proper names of individuals (a'lam ashkhas); because the totality of those rules is one thing with no multiplicity, only the expressions indicating them are multiple. And if what is meant by them is the perception or the faculty, then they are of the type of proper name of genus ('alam jins).
[Margin]
His statement: (only the expressions indicating them are multiple) i.e., and the perceptions related to them, just as the names of a single person are multiple and his conception in the mind of Zayd, 'Amr, and others is multiple.
His statement: (And if what is meant by them is the perception or the faculty, then they are of the type of proper name of genus) because their meanings then are accidents whose individuals multiply, so consideration of individuation cannot be achieved in them.
And that they are of the type of proper name of genus is what al-Sayyid, al-'Abbadi, and others have verified.
And the scholar of the ummah, my master al-Hasan al-Yusi, investigated it, with the summary: that their being proper names is refuted by their definition; for definition is only for universal concepts, and the particular proper name is not definable because, as mentioned, particulars are not defined. His student, the investigator Ibn Zakari, transmitted his full words in his commentary on 'al-Farida'. And some investigators answered this investigation with the summary: that the proper name of genus is considered from two aspects: 1. from the aspect of its mental presence, and from this aspect its definition cannot be achieved. 2. And it is considered not from the aspect of its mental presence but from its essence as such, and with this consideration it is a universal thing and thus defined. So if you are asked: 'What is al-mantiq?' the answer does not come from the first aspect, because he is ignorant, so the situation compels that he be answered with the essence as such, and when it is present in the mind of the hearer, you point to it from the aspect of its presence in his mind and say to him: 'This is al-mantiq.' End.
•
And Ibn Abi Sharif, al-'Abbadi, and Ibn Zakari have spoken on the issue in the commentary on 'al-Farida', and he extended to about four folios, so look at it if you wish.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage resolves the dispute about whether names of sciences like 'al-mantiq' and 'al-nahw' are proper names of individuals (a'lam ashkhas) or proper names of genus (alam jins). The author states the truth: if what is meant by them is the specific set of rules (qawa'id makhsusa), then they are a'lam ashkhas because that set is a single entity with no multiplicity; only the expressions referring to it are multiple. But if what is meant is the perception (idrak) or the faculty (malaka) of that science, then they are from the type of alam jins, because these are accidents with multiple individuals. The margin commentary explains that the multiplicity of expressions and perceptions does not affect the singularity of the set of rules. It also cites al-Sayyid and al-'Abbadi verifying the second view. A critical investigation by al-Hasan al-Yusi raises the objection that if these names are proper names (a'lam), they cannot be defined, because definition applies only to universals. The response is that alam jins can be considered from two aspects: as mental presence (in which case it cannot be defined) and as an essence (which is universal and definable). The commentator suggests referring to longer discussions by Ibn Abi Sharif and others.

الملكة
التعريف
علم الجنس

Refining the Term for Accident in Definitions
Commentator corrects a wording in the matn: 'aariḍ' should be 'araḍ'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the terms 'aariḍ' and 'araḍ' in logical definitions and identify when a commentator is refining a matn's terminology.

Core Idea
Precision in logical terms is not pedantry; a single letter changes a definition's meaning.

In this lesson, we examine a brief but important correction by a commentator on the matn's wording. The matn used 'aariḍ' (occurring attribute) when the standard term in logic for an accident is 'araḍ'. The commentator flags this as leniency (tasāmuh) and notes that some manuscripts have the correct term. This teaches us that even well-known works require careful scrutiny of each term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problematic term in the matn
Find the term 'aariḍ' in the matn. What is its literal meaning as an active participle?
It means 'that which occurs' – implying a transient event.
2
Compare with the correct term 'araḍ'
Recall the standard term for the universal accident in logic. Contrast 'araḍ' (accident, an inherent attribute) with 'aariḍ' (occurring).
'Araḍ' is one of the five universals and is stable in meaning.
3
Understand the commentator's objection
The commentator says the phrasing implies the attribution is to the word 'aariḍ', which is not the case. The intended meaning is the accident itself.
This is a subtle point about the relationship between word and meaning.
4
Check the manuscript variant
The commentator notes that some copies read 'araḍ', which resolves the issue. This shows that scribal variants can affect logical precision.
Manuscripts are not infallible; critical reading is essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أَوْ لِعَارِضِ) فيه تسامح ؛ لأنَّه يقتضي أنَّ النِّسبة للفظ عارض حتّى يقال: وليس الأمر كذلك، ويوجد في بعض النسخ : «عرض»، ولا إشكال حينئذ.
His statement: 'or to an accidentally occurring attribute (aariḍ)' contains leniency; because it implies that the attribution is to the word 'aariḍ' so that it is said... and it is not like that. And in some copies it is found as 'araḍ' (accident), and then there is no problem.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This short passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) on the matn's wording. The matn uses 'aariḍ' (the active participle: that which occurs incidentally), but the commentator notes that this is imprecise (tasāmuh). The correct term should be 'araḍ' (accident, the inherent attribute) because the accidental attribute in a definition (rasm) is an inherent quality, not an occurring one. He points out that some manuscripts have 'araḍ' instead, which removes the issue.

Key Terms
عارض (ʻāriḍ): An attribute that occurs accidentally; the active participle. The commentator notes it is leniently used here for the accidental attribute in rasm. [Note: In logic, 'arad' (accident) is the standard term for one of the five universals; 'aariḍ' is less common and may imply a different nuance.]
عرض (ʻaraḍ): The accident; an attribute that is not essential. This is the correct term according to the commentator. [Note: Standard stable term in logic.]
تسامح (tasāmuh): Leniency or imprecision in expression. Here, the commentator critiques the matn for using a less precise term. [Note: Common metalinguistic term in commentary tradition.]

Key Takeaway

Division of Universals into Essential, Accidental, and Species
Al-Bannani defines the threefold division of universals into essential, accidental, and species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given universal as essential, accidental, or species based on its relation to the quiddity of its individuals.

Core Idea
Universals divide into three: parts of quiddity, external to quiddity, and the quiddity itself.

In this passage, Al-Bannani clarifies the foundational division of universals. This classification is crucial for understanding definitions (ḥadd and rasm). By examining the relation to quiddity, you will learn to identify which universals are essential (dhātī) and which are accidental ('araḍī), and why the species (naw') is a separate category.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
From the passage, list the three types of universals: essential (dhātī), accidental ('araḍī), and species (naw'). Note that the author says the division is threefold.
2
Understand the criterion: relation to quiddity
The classification is based on whether the universal is a part of the quiddity, external to it, or identical to it. Quiddity (māhiyyah) is what makes a thing what it is.
3
Apply the definitions to examples
For 'human', 'animal' (genus) and 'rational' (differentia) are parts of the quiddity → essential. 'Moving' (general accident) and 'laughter' (property) are external → accidental. 'Human' itself is the very quiddity → species.
The species is neither a part nor external; it is the whole.
4
Check the common misunderstanding
Some think species is essential because it belongs to quiddity, but it is the whole quiddity, not a part. Therefore it is neither essential nor accidental.
Essential always means being a part, not the whole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱) - أَنَّ أَوَّلَ القِسْمَيْنِ [ج/ ١١] السَّابِقَيْنِ وَهُوَ الكُلّيُّ : 1 - يُنْسَبُ إِلَى الذَّاتِ ; أَي : المَاهِيَّةِ، فَيُقَالُ فِيهِ : ذَاتِيٌّ إِذَا كَانَ مُنْدَرِجاً فِيهَا ; أَيْ: جُزْءاً مِنْهَا . 2 - وَيُنْسَبُ إِلَى العَرَضِ، فَيُقَالُ فِيهِ : عَرَضِيٌّ إِذَا كَانَ خَارِجاً عَنْهَا . 3 - وَمَنْهُومُهُ : أَنَّهُ إِذَا لَمْ يَكُنْ جُزْءاً مِنَ المَاهِيَّةِ وَلَا خَارِجاً مِنْهَا (1); بِأَنْ كَانَ هُوَ نَفْسُ المَاهِيَّةِ، فَلَا يُقَالُ فِيهِ : ذَاتِيٌّ وَلَا عَرَضِيُّ، وَعَلَيْهِ فَالقِسْمَةُ ثُلَاثِيَّةٌ . فَالذَّاتِيُّ هُوَ: «الكُلِّيُّ الَّذِي يَكُونُ جُزْءاً مِنْ مَاهِيَّةِ أَفْرَادِهِ»، وَيَصْدُقُ بِالجِنْسِ گ: «الحَيَوَانِ» لـ «الإِنْسَانِ ، وَبِالفَصْلِ كَـ : «النَّاطِقِ لَهُ. وَالعَرَضِيُّ هُوَ: «الكُلِّيُّ الخَارِجُ عَنْ مَاهِيَّةِ أَفْرَادِهِ، وَيَصْدُقُ بِالعَرَضِ العَامّ : «المُتَحَرِّكِ» لـ «الإِنْسَانِ ، وَبِالعَرَضِ الخَاصٌ وَيُسَمَّى : «الخَاصَّةَ» كَـ : «الصَّاحِكِ» : لِـ «الإِنْسَانِ)) . وَمَا لَيْسَ جُزْءاً وَلَا خَارِجاً هُوَ النَّوْعُ ; كَـ : الإِنْسَانِ» ; لِأَنَّهُ نَفْسُ مَاهِيَّةِ أَفْرَادِهِ.
The first of the two preceding divisions, which is the universal, is related to the essence, i.e., the quiddity, so it is said: essential if it is included in it, i.e., a part of it. And it is related to the accident, so it is said: accidental if it is external to it. And its concept: that if it is not a part of the quiddity nor external from it, in that it is the quiddity itself, then it is not called essential nor accidental, and accordingly the division is threefold. The essential is: the universal that is a part of the quiddity of its individuals, and it applies to the genus like 'animal' for 'human', and to the differentia like 'rational' for it. The accidental is: the universal external to the quiddity of its individuals, and it applies to the general accident like 'moving' for 'human', and to the special accident called 'property' like 'laughter' for 'human'. And what is neither a part nor external is the species, like 'human'; because it is the very quiddity of its individuals.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of universals based on their relation to the quiddity of individuals. The author begins by referring back to a previous division, stating that the universal can be related to the essence (quiddity) or to the accident. If it is a part of the quiddity, it is called essential (dhāti); if it is external, it is called accidental ('araḍī). There is also a third category: when the universal is neither a part nor external but identical to the quiddity itself, it is neither essential nor accidental—this is the species (naw'). Thus the division is threefold. Then he gives formal definitions: The essential is a universal that is a part of the quiddity of its individuals, exemplified by the genus 'animal' for 'human' and the differentia 'rational'. The accidental is a universal external to the quiddity, including the general accident like 'moving' and the special accident (property) like 'laughter'. The species is that which is neither part nor external, such as 'human' which is the very quiddity of its individuals. This classification is foundational in logic for definitions (ḥadd and rasm) because essential components constitute the definition, while accidental properties are not part of it.

Key Terms
ذَاتِي
عَرَضِي
نَوْع
مَاهِيَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Universals are divided based on their relation to quiddity: essential (part), accidental (external), and species (identical).

The Scope of Māhiyyah: Beyond Existence
Al-Bannani explains the term māhiyyah, its relation to dhāt, haqīqah, and its derivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish māhiyyah from haqīqah and identify whether a given term's quiddity is real or conceptual.

Core Idea
Māhiyyah includes both real and conceptual quiddities, making it broader than haqīqah.

The term 'māhiyyah' (quiddity) is foundational in logic and metaphysics. This lesson clarifies that māhiyyah applies even to non-existent conceptual objects, unlike 'haqīqah' which requires actualization. This distinction is crucial for defining terms in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and its definition
Find the phrase 'that by which the thing is itself' and note the inclusion of 'whether existent or non-existent' in the Arabic: 'معدوماً كان أو موجوداً'.
2
Compare the examples: insān vs. 'anqā'
The quiddity of 'human' is rational animal (real and existent). The quiddity of 'anqā'' is merely conceptual—only understood from its word. This shows māhiyyah covers both.
3
Distinguish māhiyyah from haqīqah
Observe that we do not say 'haqīqat al-'anqā'' because haqīqah requires actualization. Māhiyyah includes non-existent quiddities, hence is more general.
4
Understand dhāt and huwiyyah
When quiddity is actualized, it is called dhāt or haqīqah. When individuated, it is huwiyyah. Note that dhāt may also refer to the individuals that the quiddity applies to.
5
Apply the distinction
Given a term like 'phoenix' (a mythical bird), determine: does it have a māhiyyah? A haqīqah? A dhāt? Answer: it has a māhiyyah (conceptual), but no haqīqah or dhāt because it lacks external existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أي : المَاهِيَّةِ) الماهيَّةُ عند الإطلاق أعمُّ مِن أن تكون حقيقيَّةٌ أو اعتباريَّةٌ؛ إذ ماهيَّةُ الشَّيء ومائيَّتُهُ: «ما به يكون الشَّيء هو هو ; معدوماً كان أو موجوداً»، فماهية «الإنسان»: ما به يكون الإنسان إنساناً ; أعني : الحَيَوَان الناطق، وماهيَّةُ «العنقاء»: ما يتعقل ويفهم من لفظه، فالماهيَّةُ أعمُّ مِنَ الحقيقة فلا يقال ذات العنقاء ولا حقيقته بل ماهيته; أي: ما يتعقل قال في شرح المقاصده : الماهية إذا اعتبرت مع التَّحقق سمّيت : «ذاتاً) و: «حقيقة»، وإذا اعتبرت التّشخص سمّيت : «هويَّةً»، وقد يُرادُ بـ «الذَّات»: ما صدقت عليه مِنَ الأفراد . اهـ باختصار . فالماهيَّةُ لفظها مأخوذ مِن مَا هِي؟» ; لأنَّه يجاب بها عنِ السُّؤال بـ «ما هي ؟» كما كلام شارح المقاصد، وارتضاه الشيخ جسوس. وأمَّا جعل اليوسي لها منسوبة إلى ما فضعفت الألف فقيل : مائيَّة، ثمَّ قُلبت الهمزة هاء»، فخارج عن القياس.
His saying: (i.e., the quiddity). Quiddity in absolute terms is more general than being either real or mental; because the quiddity of a thing and its mā'iyyah is 'that by which the thing is itself, whether it is existent or non-existent'. So the quiddity of 'human' is that by which man is man, i.e., the rational animal, and the quiddity of 'anqā'' is that which is conceptualized and understood from its word. So quiddity is more general than reality; so one does not say 'the essence of anqā'' nor 'its reality', but rather 'its quiddity', i.e., what is conceptualized. He said in Sharh al-Maqāsid: 'When quiddity is considered with actualization, it is called dhāt and haqīqah; when considered with individuation, it is called huwiyyah; and sometimes by dhāt is meant that which its individuals apply to.' Summarized. The word māhiyyah is derived from 'mā hiya?' (what is it?), because it is answered by it to the question 'what is it?', as stated by the commentator of al-Maqāsid, and approved by Shaykh Jassūs. As for al-Yūsī making it derived from 'mā' with the weakening of the alif so it is said mā'iyyah, then the hamza is changed to hā', that is outside the analogy.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage clarifies the term māhiyyah (quiddity). The author first notes that māhiyyah is broader than haqīqah because it applies even to non-existent things like the mythical bird 'anqā', whose quiddity is merely conceptual. He then cites Sharh al-Maqāsid: when quiddity is considered with external existence, it is called dhāt or haqīqah; when considered with individuation, it is called huwiyyah (identity). The term dhāt may also refer to the individuals that the quiddity applies to. He then gives the etymology: māhiyyah comes from the question 'mā hiya?' (what is it?) with the ta' marbūtah. He rejects al-Yūsī's alternative derivation from 'mā' as being irregular. This discussion is important for understanding that quiddity is the basic concept of a thing independent of existence.

Key Terms
مَاهِيَّة
ذَات
حَقِيقَة
هَوِيَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Māhiyyah is broader than haqīqah because it includes non-existent conceptual quiddities.
C) Haqīqah is broader than māhiyyah.
D) Māhiyyah only applies to existent objects.

Key Takeaway
Māhiyyah is the quiddity of a thing considered apart from existence, making it applicable to both real and conceptual objects.

The Etymology of Māhiyyah: From Question to Term
Al-Bannani explains the etymology of māhiyyah and rejects an irregular alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the derivation of māhiyyah from the interrogative phrase 'mā hiya' and evaluate the alternative etymology proposed by al-Yūsī.

Core Idea
The standard derivation of māhiyyah is from 'mā hiya' (what is it?), not from the weakened 'mā'.

Just as the term 'māhiyyah' itself is about answering 'what is it?', its etymology reflects that role. This lesson examines the accepted derivation and a rejected alternative, showing the precision of Arabic logical terminology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the standard etymology
The text says māhiyyah is taken from the interrogative phrase 'mā hiya?' (what is it?) with the addition of the feminine suffix (ta' marbūtah).
2
Understand the reason for the derivation
Because māhiyyah is used to answer the question 'what is it?'—it defines the quiddity of a thing. This connects the term to its logical function.
3
Examine the alternative by al-Yūsī
Al-Yūsī derived it from the word 'mā' alone: 'mā' with weakened alif becomes mā'iyyah, then the hamza changes to hā', giving māhiyyah.
4
Evaluate why it is rejected
The author says this derivation 'is outside the analogy' (خارج عن القياس). It does not follow standard morphological rules for such formations.
5
Connect etymology to meaning
The accepted derivation from 'mā hiya' reinforces that māhiyyah captures the essence that answers the 'what is it?' question, making it a precise logical tool.

Etymology of 'Ma'iyyah' and the Rules of Nisbah
Author explains the derivation of 'ma'iyyah' from the interrogative 'ma' and the linguistic rules for forming the relative adjective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the morphological derivation of 'ma'iyyah' from the interrogative 'ma' and explain the phonetic changes involved.

Core Idea
The term 'ma'iyyah' is built from 'ma' exactly like 'kayfiyyah' from 'kayfa'.

The word 'ma'iyyah' (quiddity) is not arbitrary; its form reveals its function. Just as 'kayfiyyah' answers 'how?' and 'kammiyyah' answers 'how many?', 'ma'iyyah' answers 'what?'. Understanding its Arabic morphological roots helps you see why logicians chose this term and how it relates to the question about essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base word
The origin is the interrogative particle 'ma' (what). Notice that 'ma' is a two-letter word (bi-literal) ending with the weak letter alif.
2
Understand the nisbah pattern
In Arabic, to form a relative adjective (nisbah), you add a 'ya' (with kasrah) at the end. For words ending with a weak letter, special rules apply.
3
Double the original alif
The second letter (the alif) is doubled. So the base 'ma' becomes 'maa' in effect, then the second alif is turned into a hamzah, yielding 'ma'iyyah'.
The word 'ma'iyyah' is written with a hamzah (ء) on the line after the alif.
4
Consider the alternative form
The author notes that if you change the hamzah into a waw, you get 'mawiyyah', which is also permissible by the rules of nisbah for bi-literal roots with a weak final letter.
This alternative is less common but grammatically sound.
5
Connect to logical usage
Why is this term used for quiddity? Because the answer to 'what is it?' gives the essence or quiddity of a thing, exactly as 'kayfiyyah' gives its quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا المائيَّةُ : فَمَنْسُوبَةٌ إِلَى لَفْظَةِ «ما» الِاسْتِفْهَامِيَّةِ ; لِأَنَّهَا تَقَعُ فِي جَوَابِ السُّؤَالِ بِـ «ما» كَـ: الكَيْفِيَّةِ وَالْكَمِّيَّةِ لِلْمَسْؤُولِ عَنْهُ بِـ «كَيْفَ» وَ «كَمْ» . وَضُوعِفَتِ الْأَلِفُ فَقُلِبَتِ الثَّانِيَةُ هَمْزَةً فَقِيلَ : «مَائِيَّةٌ» ; وَلَوْ قُلِبَتِ الْهَمْزَةُ وَاوًا فَقِيلَ : «مَاوِيَّةٌ» لِجَازَ عَلَى قَاعِدَةِ النَّسَبِ فِي الثُّنَائِيِّ الَّذِي آخِرُهُ لَيْنٌ .
And as for 'al-ma'iyyah': it is attributed to the word 'ma' interrogative; because it occurs in the answer to the question with 'ma', like 'al-kayfiyyah' (quality) and 'al-kammiyyah' (quantity) for the thing asked about with 'kayfa' and 'kam'. And the alif was doubled, then the second was turned into a hamzah, so it was said: 'ma'iyyah'; and if the hamzah were turned into a waw, so it was said: 'mawiyyah', it would be permissible according to the rule of attribution for nouns with two letters whose final is a weak letter.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'ma'iyyah' (quiddity) by its linguistic derivation. It is the relative adjective (mansubah) from the interrogative word 'ma' (what), because the answer to 'what is it?' gives the quiddity, just as 'kayfiyyah' (quality) answers 'how?' and 'kammiyyah' (quantity) answers 'how many?'. The base word 'ma' has two letters; when forming the relative adjective, the alif (which is the second letter) is doubled, and then the second alif is changed into a hamzah, resulting in 'ma'iyyah'. Alternatively, if one changes the hamzah into a waw, the form 'mawiyyah' is also permissible by the rules of forming attributive adjectives from bilateral roots ending in a weak letter (like 'ma' where the final vowel is alif).

Key Terms
مائية
ما الاستفهامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ma'iyyah is the relative adjective of the interrogative 'ma' because it answers the question about essence, following Arabic morphological rules.

The First Warning: Confusion Between Essential and Accidental
Author warns that the conceptual distinction between essential and accidental is often confused in actual instances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential (dhati) from accidental ('aradi) in specific cases and recognize why the differentia and property can be confused in practice.

Core Idea
The conceptual distinction is clean; the practical application is messy.

In this passage, the commentator warns that although you have learned the conceptual difference between essential and accidental, real-world instances often blur the line. This leads even sharp minds to confuse differentia with property. The commentator uses the example of 'rational' vs 'laughing' for humans to highlight the issue.

Guided Path
1
Recall the conceptual definitions
Essential is that which is part of the quiddity (mahiyya) of a thing, such as genus and differentia. Accidental is that which is outside the quiddity, including property and general accident.
Know the key terms: dhati, 'aradi, fasl, khassa.
2
Apply to a specific case: 'rational' for human
'Rational' is essential to 'human' because it is part of the definition: human is a rational animal. It is the differentia (fasl).
Definition (hadd) uses genus and differentia.
3
Apply to another case: 'laughing' for human
'Laughing' is accidental to 'human' because it is not part of the quiddity; it is a property (khassa) that follows from the essential nature. A description (rasm) uses genus and property.
The distinction between hadd and rasm hinges on whether the distinguishing feature is essential or accidental.
4
Understand the confusion
In practice, one may not know whether a given universal is inside the essence or outside it. This leads to confusion between differentia and property, and some claim the distinction is merely terminological.
The commentator highlights that even the most intelligent can be confused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيهان: (۱) - الأَوَّلُ : قَدْ عَلِمْتَ الفَرْقَ بَيْنَ الذَّاتِي وَالعَرَضِي مِنْ حَيْثُ مَفْهُومُهُمَا ، وَكَثِيراً مَا يَلْتَبِسَانِ مِنْ حَيْثُ المَصْدُوقُ، وَمِنْ هُنَا يَشْكُلُ الفَرْقُ بَيْنَ الفَصْلِ وَالخَاصَّةِ، حَتَّى زَعَمَ بَعْضُهُمْ أَنَّ تَفْرِيقَهُمْ بَيْنَ النَّاطِقِ وَالصَّاحِكِ حَيْثُ جَعَلُوا الأَوَّلَ فَضْلاً وَالتَّعْرِيفَ بِهِ حَدًّا ، وَالثَّانِي خَاصَّةٌ وَالتَّعْرِيفَ بِهِ رَسْماً إِنَّمَا هُوَ اصْطِلاحِيٌّ فَقَط . قوله : (وَكَثِيراً مَا يَلْتَبِسَانِ مِنْ حَيْثُ المَصْدُوقُ) أي : بأن لا يدري في بعض الكليات أهو داخل في الماهية فيحكم عليه بأنه ذاتي، أو خارج فيحكم عليه بأنَّه عرضي؟ إذ كثيراً ما يشتبه الجنس بالعَرَض العام، والفصلُ بالخاصَّة على الأذكياء النَّحارير فضلاً عن غيرهم.
Two warnings: (1) The first: You have known the difference between the essential and the accidental in terms of their concept, but often they are confused in terms of the referent. Hence the difference between the differentia and the property becomes problematic, to the extent that some claim that their distinction between 'rational' and 'laughing' — where they made the first a differentia and definition by it a definition, and the second a property and definition by it a description — is only terminological. His statement: 'And often they are confused in terms of the referent' meaning: that one does not know in some universals whether it is inside the essence so he judges it as essential, or outside so he judges it as accidental. For often the genus is confused with the general accident, and the differentia with the property, even among sharp-witted intelligent people, let alone others.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage is a warning (tanbih) from the commentator about a common confusion in logic. The author states that although the conceptual distinction between essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) is clear, in practice they are often confused when applied to real things (al-masduq). Consequently, the distinction between differentia (fasl) and property (khassa) becomes unclear. Some have even claimed that the differentiation between 'rational' (natiq) as a differentia, giving a definition (hadd), and 'laughing' (dahik) as a property, giving a description (rasm), is merely terminological. The commentator then elaborates on the phrase 'often confused in terms of referent', explaining that the difficulty lies in determining whether a given universal belongs to the essence (mahiyya) of a thing or is outside it. This confusion happens because genus can be mistaken for general accident ('arad 'amm) and differentia for property, even by the most intelligent scholars.

Key Terms
ذاتي
عرضي
فصل
خاصة
حد
رسم

Key Takeaway
The conceptual distinction between essential and accidental is clear, but in practice, identifying which universals are essential requires careful analysis, and differentia can be mistaken for property.

Three Differences Between Essential and Accidental Attributes
Author explains three classical distinctions between essential and accidental attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish any attribute as essential (dhati) or accidental ('aradi) using the three criteria of understandability, causality, and removability.

Core Idea
Three tests tell if an attribute shapes the essence itself or merely accompanies it.

In Islamic logic, definitions must capture the essence (mahiyya) of a thing. The key to a correct definition is distinguishing attributes that are essential (constitutive) from those that are accidental (concomitant). This lesson unpacks the three classical differences between essential and accidental attributes, attributed to the ancients and cited by Imam al-Ghazali. Mastering these criteria will allow you to evaluate definitions in any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Apply the first difference: Understandability
Check whether the essence can be understood without conceiving the attribute. For example, you cannot understand 'man' without understanding 'rational', but you can understand 'man' without understanding 'laughing'. So 'rational' is essential, 'laughing' is accidental.
This is the most fundamental test; if an attribute is essential, the essence is inconceivable without it.
2
Apply the second difference: Causality
Essential attributes are not caused by anything external; they are intrinsic. Accidental attributes have a cause. For instance, 'rational' is not caused, but 'laughing' is caused by wonder. Therefore, 'rational' is essential, 'laughing' is accidental.
If you can ask 'Why does this thing have this attribute?' and the answer points to something else, it is accidental.
3
Apply the third difference: Removability
Imagine removing the attribute while keeping the essence. If the essence disappears, it is essential; if the essence remains, it is accidental. For example, if you mentally remove 'rational' from man, man ceases to be man. If you remove 'laughing', man remains what he is. This confirms the first two tests.
This third difference is a consequence of the first: what cannot be understood without the essence cannot be removed without destroying the essence.

Source Text

فصل في مباحث الألفاظ وَلَيْسَ كَمَا زَعَمَ ، فَإِنَّ كَوْنَ الشَّيْءِ جُزْءاً مِنَ الحَقِيقَةِ، أَوْ خَارِجَا عَنْهَا أَمْرٌ ثَابِتٌ فِي الوَاقِعِ لَيْسَ رَاجِعاً لِلاصْطِلاحِ . نَعَمْ؛ التَّسْمِيَةُ بِالفَصْلِ وَالخَاصَّةِ أَمْرُ اصْطِلاحِيٌّ ، وَلَكِنْ لَيْسَ الكَلَامُ فِيهَا ، وَقَدْ فَرَّقَ بَيْنَهُمَا القُدَمَاءُ بِثَلَاثَةِ فُرُوقٍ : الأَوَّلُ : أَنَّ الذَّاتِي هُوَ الَّذِي لَا يُمْكِنُ فَهْمُ الذَّاتِ بِدُونِهِ، وَالعَرَضِيَّ بِخِلَافِهِ . الثَّانِي: أَنَّ الذَّاتِي هُوَ الَّذِي لَا يُعَلَّلُ، وَالعَرَضِيَّ يُعَلَّلُ. قوله : (وَلَيْسَ كَمَا زَعَمَ ليس ما فهمه هذا الشَّارح هو مراد هذا القائل، كما أفصح عن ذلك شيخنا المحقق سيّدي الطَّيِّب، فقال : إنَّ مراد هذا القائل أنَّهم لم يتحقَّقوا أنَّ الناطق جزء الحقيقة، والضاحك خارج عنها بحسب الواقع ونفس الأمر، فاصطلحوا على اعتقاد أنَّ الناطق داخل والضاحك خارج قصداً للتَّقريب والتَّمثيل، وليس مراد هذا القائل : أنَّ كون الشَّيء ذاتيًّا أو عَرَضب هو بحسب الاصطلاح ؛ إذ لا يقول به مميز فضلاً عن متميز . قوله : (وَقَدْ فَرَّقَ . .. إلخ) هذه الفروق الثلاثة ذكرها الإمام أبو حامد الغزالي في كتاب «مقاصد الفلاسفة»، انظر نصَّه في حواشي أبي حفص على «المختصر». إِنَّمَا قوله : (أَنَّ الذَّاتِي هُوَ الَّذِي لَا يُمْكِنُ ... إلخ) قال أبو علي بن سينا : الذاتي ما إذا فهم معناه وأخطر بالبال وفهم معنى ما هو ذاتي له وأخطر بالبال معه لم يمكن أن نفهم ذات الموصوف إلَّا بعد فهم ذلك المعنى أوَّلاً كـ الإنسان والحيوان، فإنَّك إذا فهمت ما الحيوان، وفهمت ما الإنسان، فلا تفهم الإنسان إلا وقد فهمت أولاً أنَّه حيوان، وأما ما ليس ذاتيا فقد تفهم ذات الموصوف مجرَّداً دونه ك كونه أبيض، أو موجوداً مثلاً . قوله : (أَنَّ الذَّاتِي هُوَ الَّذِي لَا يُعَلَّلُ) أي : لا يثبت لما هو ذاتي له بعِلَّةٍ، فلا تقول: «لِمَ كان الإنسان حيواناً أو ناطقاً ؟»؛ لأنَّ الإنسان هو كذلك، فكأنَّك قلت: «لِمَ كان الإنسان إنساناً؟». وكذا : السَّواد مثلاً لون لذاته، لا لشيء آخر يجعله لوناً ، فلا تقول: «لم كان لوناً؟»، بخلاف الضحك، فإنَّه يثبت للإنسان بعلَّةٍ . الثَّالِثُ : أَنَّ الذَّاتِي هُوَ الَّذِي لَا تَبْقَى الذَّاتُ مَعَ تَوَهُم رَفْعِهِ، وَالعَرَضِيَّ بِخِلَافِهِ؛ مِثَالُهُ : النَّاطِقُ ، فَإِنَّهُ لَا يُمْكِنُ فَهُمُ مَاهِيَّةِ الإِنْسَانِ بِدُونِهِ، وَيُمْكِنُ فَهْمُهَا بِدُونِ «ضَاحِكِ»، كَمَا أَنَّهُ إِذَا ثَبَتَ لَهَا بِلَا عِلَّةٍ، وَالصَّاحِكُ مَعْلُولٌ بِالتَّعَجُبِ المَعْلُولِ بِإِدْرَاكِ الغَرَائِبِ المَسْبُوقِ بِمُطْلَقِ الإِدْرَاكِ المَسْبُوقِ بِالقُوَّةِ العَاقِلَةِ الَّتِي هِيَ النَّاطِقِيَّةُ، وَكَمَا أَنَّهُ لَا يُمْكِنُ تَوَهُمُ رَفْعِ النَّاطِقِ مَعَ بَقَاءِ حَقِيقَةِ الإِنْسَانِ، بِخِلَافِ الصَّاحِكِ . وَقَدْ أَشَارَ ابْنُ الحَاجِبِ فِي أَصْلِيّهِ إِلَى الأَوَّلَيْنِ بِقَوْلِهِ : «الذَّاتِيُّ : مَا لَا يُتَصَوَّرُ فَهْمُ الذَّاتِ قَبْلَ فَهْمِهِ كَـ: اللَّوْنِيَّةِ لِلسَّوَادِ، وَالجِسْمِيَّةِ لِلإِنْسَانِ، وَقَدْ يُعَرَّفُ : بِأَنَّهُ غَيْرُ مُعَلَّلٍ وَبِالتَّرْتِيبِ العَقْلِيِّ» . اهـ (ا) . قصارة . قوله : (الثَّالِثُ : أَنَّ الذَّاتِي ... إلخ) الظَّاهِرُ : أنَّ هذا الثَّالث لازمٌ عنِ الأَوَّل؛ لأنَّه يلزم من كون الشَّيء لا تعقل الذَّات بدونه أن لا تبقى الذات مع قطع النظر عنه، فلو أتى بدله بالفرق الثالث الذي أتى به ابن الحاجب الآتي بعد، لكان أولى. قوله : (المَسْبُوقِ بِالقُوَّةِ العَاقِلَةِ اعترضه بعضهم : بأنَّ الصَّواب إسقاط هذه المرتبة؛ لاقتضائها أنَّ مطلق الإدراك متسبّب عنِ القوَّة الناطقة، وليس كذلك ؛ لأنَّ البهائم عندها مطلق الإدراك من غير القوة المدركة. وفيه نظر؛ لأنَّ الشَّارح رحمه الله تعالى تكلَّم على مطلق الإدراك المتّصف به الإنسان؛ بدليل: أنَّه جعله سبباً لإدراك الغرائب، السبب في التَّعجب الذي هو سبب في الضحك، ولا شكّ أنَّ مطلق الإدراك بهذا المعنى ناشئ عن القوَّة الناطقة، فصح ما قاله الشارح. قوله : (وَقَدْ أَشَارَ ابْنُ الحَاجِبِ فِي أَصْلِيهِ») «أصليه» بالياء المشدّدة، ومراده: «مختصره الأصلي أي : المنسوب إلى الأصول، وذلك أنَّ ابن الحاجب له مختصران: أحدهما في الفروع، والآخر في الأصول، فنبَّه الشارح هنا على أنه ذكر ما ذكر في مختصره في الأصول». وابن الحاجب هو: جمال الدين أبو عمرو عثمان بن أبي بكر الكردي الإسناوي ثمَّ المصري المالكي الفقيه المقرئ النَّحوي الأصوليُّ ، صاحب التّصانيف البديعة، وكان أبوه حاجباً للأمير عزّ الدين موسك الصَّلاحي، فقرأ القراءات على الغزنوي والشَّاطبي، كان من أركان الدين في العلم
Chapter on Topics of Words. And it is not as he claimed, for the thing being a part of the essence or outside it is a matter established in reality, not referring to convention. Yes; naming by differentia and property is a conventional matter, but the discussion is not about that. The ancients distinguished between them with three differences: The first: that the essential is that which the understanding of the essence is impossible without it, and the accidental is opposite to it. The second: that the essential is that which is not caused, and the accidental is caused. His saying: (And it is not as he claimed): what this commentator understood is not the intent of that speaker, as our teacher the investigator Sayyid al-Tayyib clarified, saying: The intent of that speaker is that they were not certain that 'rational' is part of the essence and 'laughing' is outside it in reality and in fact, so they agreed to believe that 'rational' is inside and 'laughing' is outside for the purpose of approximation and example, and it is not the intent of that speaker that the thing being essential or accidental is based on convention; for no discerning person would say that, let alone one who is distinguished. His saying: (And they distinguished... etc.): These three differences were mentioned by Imam Abu Hamid al-Ghazali in his book 'Maqasid al-Falasifa'; see his text in the glosses of Abu Hafs on 'al-Mukhtasar'. Indeed. His saying: (that the essential is that which cannot... etc.): Abu Ali ibn Sina said: The essential is that when its meaning is understood and brought to mind, and the meaning of that which is essential to it is understood and brought to mind with it, it is impossible to understand the essence of the described except after understanding that meaning first, like 'human' and 'animal': for if you understand what 'animal' is and understand what 'human' is, you do not understand 'human' except after having first understood that it is animal. As for that which is not essential, you may understand the essence of the described stripped of it, like its being white or existing for example. His saying: (that the essential is that which is not caused): i.e., it is not established for that which is essential to it by a cause, so you do not say: 'Why is man an animal or rational?' because man is so; it is as if you said: 'Why is man man?' Similarly, blackness for example is a color by its essence, not by something else that makes it a color, so you do not say: 'Why is it a color?', unlike laughter, for it is established for man by a cause. The third: that the essential is that the essence does not remain with the supposition of its removal, and the accidental is opposite to it; its example: 'rational', for it is impossible to understand the quiddity of man without it, and it is possible to understand it without 'laughing', just as it is established for it without a cause, and 'laughing' is caused by wonder, which is caused by perception of marvels, preceded by absolute perception, preceded by the rational power which is rationality. And just as it is impossible to suppose the removal of 'rational' while the reality of man remains, unlike 'laughing'. And Ibn al-Hajib in his 'Asli' indicated the first two with his statement: 'The essential: that which the understanding of the essence is not conceived before understanding it, like 'colorness' for blackness and 'bodiliness' for man, and it may be defined as that which is not caused and by the intellectual ordering.' End. Marginal note: His saying: (The third: that the essential... etc.): Apparently, this third follows from the first, because from the fact that the essence is not understood without it, it follows that the essence does not remain when it is disregarded. So if he had replaced it with the third difference that Ibn al-Hajib brings later, it would have been better. His saying: (preceded by the rational power): Some objected to it, saying: The correct thing is to omit this level, because it implies that absolute perception is caused by the rational power, and it is not so, because animals have absolute perception without the perceiving power. But there is consideration in this, because the commentator (may Allah have mercy on him) spoke about absolute perception as possessed by man, as evidenced by the fact that he made it a cause for perceiving marvels, which is the cause for wonder, which is the cause for laughter, and there is no doubt that absolute perception in this sense arises from the rational power, so what the commentator said is correct. His saying: (And Ibn al-Hajib indicated in his 'Asli'): 'Asli' with a double 'y', and he means his 'Mukhtasar al-Asli', i.e., the one attributed to principles (usul). For Ibn al-Hajib has two abridgments: one in furu' (branches) and the other in usul (principles). The commentator here pointed out that he mentioned what he mentioned in his abridgment on principles. And Ibn al-Hajib is Jamal al-Din Abu 'Amr 'Uthman ibn Abi Bakr al-Kurdi al-Isna'awi, then al-Misri, al-Maliki, the jurist, reciter, grammarian, and usuli, author of excellent works. His father was a chamberlain for Prince 'Izz al-Din Musk al-Salahi. He studied recitations under al-Ghaznawi and al-Shatibi. He was a pillar of religion in knowledge.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a logical text discussing the distinction between essential (dhati) and accidental (aradi) attributes. The author begins by rejecting a claim that the classification of something as part of the essence or outside it is merely conventional; he asserts it is a real matter. He then notes that the practice of naming differentia (fasl) and property (khassa) is conventional, but the discussion is about the underlying reality. The main content is the three classical differences between essential and accidental attributes, attributed to the ancients (qudama') and cited from al-Ghazali. The first difference: the essential is that without which the essence cannot be understood; accidental does not have this property. The second: essential is uncaused; accidental has a cause. The third: the essence cannot remain when the essential is supposed removed, while it can with accidental. The passage also includes a marginal note questioning whether the third difference is necessary given the first, and a defense of the commentator's causal chain for laughter involving the rational power. Finally, it references Ibn al-Hajib's definition in his usul work. Throughout, examples are given: 'rational' is essential to man, 'laughing' is accidental. Essential attributes like animality and rationality are intrinsic, such that man cannot be conceived without them; accidental attributes like laughter are extrinsic and require a cause.

الخاصة

Checkpoint
According to the three differences mentioned in the passage, which of the following is an essential attribute of a human?
A) Laughing
B) Being white
C) Rationality
D) Being tall

Key Takeaway
The three differences — understandability, causality, and removability — provide a reliable method to distinguish any essential attribute from an accidental one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the attribute 'bodiliness' (jismiyya) for a human, apply the three differences to determine whether it is essential or accidental. Then do the same for 'being a writer' (kitaba).
?
In Usul al-Fiqh, the definition of 'contract' (aqd) includes 'offer and acceptance' as essential elements. Use the first difference to explain why 'writing' is not essential to a contract.

Al-Tusi's Objection to the Third Definition and Its Refutation
Author presents al-Tusi's objection that some accidental concomitants share the property of removal with essentials, and refutes it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential from accidental properties by applying the criterion of 'removal via abstraction' versus 'removal via negation' as refined by al-Tusi's objection.

Core Idea
Not every property whose negation destroys the concept is essential; only those for which mere abstraction also destroys it.

Al-Tusi raises a powerful objection to the third definition of the essential (al-dhati): some accidental concomitants, like evenness for the number two, seem to share the property that their removal destroys the concept. If we remove evenness from two, two ceases to exist. Does this make evenness essential? The author's refutation hinges on a crucial distinction: 'removal' can mean either negation or mere abstraction. Understanding this distinction sharpens our ability to identify true essences.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Tusi's objection
Locate the claim: al-Tusi says that some accidental concomitants (al-lawazim al-'aradiyya) share with essential properties the characteristic that assuming their removal destroys the concept of the thing. The examples are evenness for two and oddness for three.
Remember that 'removal' here is ambiguous.
2
Understand the two interpretations of 'assumption of removal'
The author clarifies: if 'assumption of removal' means 'supposition of its negation' (farḍ intifā'ih), then the objection applies. But if it means merely 'abstracting from it' (qat' al-nazar), then it does not apply.
Negation removes the property; abstraction sets it aside without denying it.
3
Apply the abstraction test to evenness and two
Consider the concept of 'two' as the double of one. If we abstract from (set aside) the property of being evenly divisible, we can still conceive of 'double of one'. Thus the essence of two remains under abstraction. This shows evenness is not essential.
For a true essential, abstraction would also destroy the concept.
4
Contrast with a genuine essential property
Think of a true essential like 'rationality' for 'human'. If you abstract rationality from the concept of human, the concept collapses. That is because rationality is part of the quiddity, not merely an accidental concomitant.
This contrast solidifies the criterion.

Source Text

وَقَدِ اعْتَرَضَ الطُّوْسِيُّ عَلَى الأَخِيرَيْنِ : بِأَنَّ مِنَ اللَّوَازِمِ العَرَضِيَّةِ مَا يُشَارِكُ الذَّاتِيَّ فِيهِمَا : الزَّوْجِيَّةِ لِلِاثْنَيْنِ، وَالفَرْدِيَّةِ لِلثَّلَاثَةِ، وَهَذَا إِنَّمَا يَتَّجِهُ فِي الثَّالِثِ إِنْ أُرِيدَ بِتَوَهُم رَفْعِهِ فَرْضُ انْتِفَائِهِ؛ أَمَّا إِنْ أُرِيدَ بِهِ مُجَرَّدَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنْهُ ، فَلَا يَتَّجِهُ؛ لِأَنَّهُ إِذَا تَصَوَّرْنَا ضِعْفَ الوَاحِدِ مَثَلاً ، وَقَطَعْنَا النَّظَرَ عَنِ انْقِسَامِهِ بِمُتَسَاوِيَيْنِ لَمْ يَلْزَمْ مِنْ ذَلِكَ سُقُوطُ تَصَوُّرِ كُنْهِ الاثْنَيْنِ، بِخِلَافِ الذَّاتِي.
And al-Tusi objected to the last two (definitions) that among accidental concomitants are those that share with the essential in both: evenness for two, and oddness for three. And this applies only to the third (definition) if by 'assumption of its removal' is meant the supposition of its negation; but if by it is meant merely abstracting from it, then it does not apply, because if we conceive of the double of one, for example, and abstract from its division into two equal parts, it does not necessarily follow from that the lapse of conceiving the quiddity of two, contrary to the essential.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage presents a critical objection by al-Tusi against the third definition of the essential (dhati) and the definition by intellectual order (al-tartib al-'aqli). Al-Tusi argues that some accidental concomitants (like evenness for two and oddness for three) share the same property as essentials: they cannot be removed without affecting the essence. For instance, if you remove evenness from two, two ceases to exist. The response clarifies that the objection only applies if 'assumption of removal' means assuming the negation of the property. However, if it means merely abstracting from it (cutting off consideration), then the objection fails. For example, if we conceive of 'double of one' and abstract from its division into two equal parts, we do not lose the concept of two in its essence. Thus, evenness is not essential because its removal in the sense of abstraction does not destroy the essence of two, unlike genuine essentials. This refines the understanding of the third definition and distinguishes between essential and accidental properties with greater precision.

اللَّوَازِم العَرَضِيَّة
الزَّوْجِيَّة
الفَرْدِيَّة
قَطْع النَّظَر

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of al-Tusi's objection, why is evenness not an essential property of two?
A) Because evenness is not a necessary property of two
B) Because one can conceive of two even if evenness is negated
C) Because one can conceive of two without evenness by abstracting from it
D) Because evenness is caused by something else

Key Takeaway
The crucial distinction between 'removal by negation' and 'removal by abstraction' resolves al-Tusi's objection and refines the definition of the essential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the property 'being temporally originated' (huduth) for the concept 'world' (al-‘alam). Is it essential or accidental? Apply the abstraction test: can you conceive of the world without temporality? If so, what kind of property is it?
?
In a legal analogy: the property 'intoxication' is considered the illa for prohibition of wine. Is intoxication essential to the ruling? Test by abstraction: if you abstract from intoxication in the concept of wine, does the ruling of prohibition persist? Why or why not?

Limiting Tusi's Objection on Accidental Concomitants
Author limits Tusi's objection to one interpretation of 'imagine its removal'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two interpretations of 'imagine its removal' (raf') and identify which one makes Tusi's objection apply.

Core Idea
Tusi's objection only works if 'removal' means the concomitant's absence, not mere disregard.

Tusi raised a challenge against the distinction between essential and accidental concomitants, pointing out that some accidentals are as inseparable as essentials. The author of this passage offers a crucial nuance: the objection only holds under one interpretation of 'imagine its removal.' Understanding this distinction is key to preserving the logical difference between essential and accidental properties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core of Tusi's objection
Tusi objects to the last two types of accidentals by noting that some accidentals (like evenness for two) seem just as inseparable as essentials. Find where the author states this.
Look for the phrase 'أَنَّ مِنَ اللَّوَازِمِ العَرَضِيَّةِ مَا يُشَارِكُ الذَّاتِيَّ فِيهِمَا'.
2
Locate the two interpretations of 'raf''
The author distinguishes two meanings: فرض انتفائه (assuming its absence) and مجرد قطع النظر عنه (mere disregard). Identify the Arabic phrases.
The distinction is signaled by 'إِنْ أُرِيدَ... أَمَّا إِنْ أُرِيدَ...'
3
Apply the first interpretation
If raf' means assuming the concomitant's absence, then two cannot be conceived without evenness, so Tusi's objection holds.
Under this interpretation, the concomitant is as inseparable as an essential.
4
Apply the second interpretation
If raf' means merely disregarding the property, then you can conceive two without considering evenness, so the objection fails.
Disregarding is possible for accidentals but not for essentials.
5
Compare with essential properties
For essentials, even disregarding is impossible; you cannot conceive a human without animality, even in disregard. This confirms the difference.
This contrast is implied by 'بِخِلَافِ الذَّاتِي'.

وَقَدِ اعْتَرَضَ الطُّوْسِيُّ عَلَى الأَخِيرَيْنِ : بِأَنَّ مِنَ اللَّوَازِمِ العَرَضِيَّةِ مَا يُشَارِكُ الذَّاتِيَّ فِيهِمَا : الزَّوْجِيَّةِ لِلِاثْنَيْنِ، وَالفَرْدِيَّةِ لِلثَّلَاثَةِ، وَهَذَا إِنَّمَا يَتَّجِهُ فِي الثَّالِثِ إِنْ أُرِيدَ بِتَوَهُم رَفْعِهِ فَرْضُ انْتِفَائِهِ ؛ أَمَّا إِنْ أُرِيدَ بِهِ مُجَرَّدَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنْهُ ، فَلَا يَتَّجِهُ؛ لِأَنَّهُ إِذَا تَصَرَّرْنَا ضِعْفَ الوَاحِدِ مَثَلاً، وَقَطَعْنَا النَّظَرَ عَنِ انْقِسَامِهِ بِمُتَسَاوِبَيْنِ لَمْ يَلْزَمْ مِنْ ذَلِكَ يُطْلَانُ تَصَوُّرٍ كُنْهَ الاثْنَيْنِ، بِخِلَافِ الذَّاتِي
And Tusi objected to the last two: that among accidental concomitants there are those that share with the essential in both (the two properties): being even for two, and being odd for three. And this (objection) only applies to the third if by 'imagine its removal' is meant the assumption of its absence. But if by it is meant merely disregarding it, then it does not apply; because if we imagine the double of one, for example, and disregard its division into two equal parts, the attainment of the conceptualization of the quiddity of two does not follow, unlike the essential.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is a response to Tusi's objection concerning accidental concomitants (lawāzim 'araḍiyya). Tusi noted that some accidentals, like evenness for two and oddness for three, appear to have the same property as essentials: they cannot be removed without destroying the subject. The author argues that Tusi's objection only targets the third species of accidentals (the inseparable but non-defining ones) under one interpretation of 'imagine its removal.' The author distinguishes two meanings of raf' (removal): (1) assuming the actual nonexistence of the concomitant, and (2) merely disregarding it (qaṭ' al-naẓar). If 'removal' means assuming absence, then the objection applies because two cannot be conceived as not even. But if it means disregarding the property, you can think of two without considering its evenness, whereas you cannot disregard essential parts like animality for human. Thus the objection fails for the second meaning. This nuanced point shows that accidental concomitants, even if inseparable, are not essential because they can be disregarded in conceptualization.

Key Terms
اللازم العرضي
الذاتي
رفع
تصور

Checkpoint

A) Disregarding the concomitant while conceiving the subject
B) Assuming the actual absence of the concomitant
C) Both disregarding and assuming absence
D) Neither disregarding nor assuming absence

Key Takeaway
Tusi's objection to the distinction between essential and accidental concomitants is neutralized by clarifying that 'imagine its removal' can mean either assuming absence or disregarding—only the former makes the objection apply.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take Tusi's example of evenness for two. Apply both interpretations of 'imagine its removal' and explain why the objection fails under one interpretation.
?
Consider the accidental concomitant 'being drinkable' for water. Under which interpretation would Tusi's objection apply, and why? Use the author's reasoning.

Al-Qarāfī's Two Methods to Identify Essential Parts
Al-Qarāfī's two methods to identify essential parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply al-Qarāfī's two methods (linguistic/customary transmission and rational assumption) to determine whether a given property is essential or accidental in an Islamic concept.

Core Idea
Essential parts are known either by established definition or by rational assumption.

In this lesson, we explore al-Qarāfī's practical guidance for distinguishing essential (dhātī) from accidental. He offers two distinct methods: one relying on transmitted usage (naql) from language, law, or custom, and the other relying on intellectual assumption. Understanding these methods is crucial for constructing sound definitions in logic and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept
Pick an Islamic concept (e.g., salat) and list its possible essential parts based on your knowledge.
For example, for salat, possible parts: intention, takbir, ruku', sujud, etc.
2
Apply the first method
Check if there is a linguistic, legal, or customary definition that specifies which parts are included in the meaning of the word. Use reliable transmission (naql) to determine the established coinage.
E.g., the legal definition of salat is 'specific actions starting with takbir and ending with taslim'.
3
Apply the second method
If no established definition exists, rationally assume a minimal composite essence for the concept. Whatever is beyond those assumed parts is accidental.
E.g., for salat, if you rationally assume essence is 'intention + takbir + ruku' + sujud', then other actions like qira'ah might be accidental if not universally agreed.
4
Compare and conclude
Determine which method applies, and then decide whether a given property is essential or accidental. Justify your conclusion.
Both methods can converge or one may be absent. If both agree, it is strong evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَدْ ذَكَرَ القَرَافِيُّ : أَنَّ الذَّاتِيَّ يُتَبَيَّنُ بِأَحَدٍ طَرِيقَيْنِ : أَحَدُهُمَا : أَنْ يُعْلَمَ مِنَ اللُّغَةِ أَنَّ اللَّفْظَ وُضِعَ لِأَمْرَيْنِ مَثَلاً ، فَيُعْلَمَ أَنَّ كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا دَاخِل فِي المُسَمَّى ، وَأَنَّ مَا عَدَاهُمَا خَارِجٌ عَنْهُ ، وَكَذَا الوَضْعُ الشَّرْعِيُّ كَالصَّلَاةِ، وَالعُرْفِيُّ كَالحَالِ عِنْدَ التَّحَاةِ، وَيُعْرَفُ ذَلِكَ بِالنَّقْلِ . الثَّانِي : أَنْ يَفْرُضَ العَقْلُ حَقِيقَةٌ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنْ شَيْئَيْنِ، فَيَكُونُ مَا عَدَاهُمَا خَارِجًا عَنْهَا .
And al-Qarāfī mentioned that the essential is made evident by one of two methods: The first is that it be known from language that the word was coined for two things, for example, so it is known that each of them is included in the named, and that what is beyond them is external to it; and similarly the legal coinage like prayer, and the customary as the state of greeting; and that is known through transmission. The second: that the intellect assumes a reality composed of two things, then what is beyond them is external to it.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage presents al-Qarāfī's two methods for identifying essential parts (dhātī) of a concept. The first method relies on established usage: language (e.g., 'human' coined for 'rational animal'), Islamic law (e.g., 'prayer' coined for specific actions), or custom (e.g., 'greeting' coined for a specific phrase). These definitions are known through reliable transmission (naql). The second method is purely intellectual: you suppose an essence composed of certain elements, and whatever is not included is accidental. For example, if you rationally posit that man is composed of animality and rationality, then other attributes like laughter are external. This method is used when no established definition exists. The passage thus provides a practical guide for logicians and jurists to distinguish essential from accidental properties, a key step in constructing definitions and arguments.

Key Terms
الذاتي
الوضع
النقل

Key Takeaway

Al-Qarafi's Two Methods: Wad' and I'tibar
Author explains al-Qarafi's two methods to distinguish essential from accidental attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply al-Qarafi's two methods (wad' and i'tibar) to identify essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) attributes in definitions from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Two tools—convention and reason—separate what belongs to the essence from what is external.

How do we know which attributes are truly essential to a concept and which are merely accidental? Al-Qarafi provides two systematic methods: the first relies on established convention (wad') in language, law, or custom; the second relies on rational analysis (i'tibar) of the concept itself. These methods are crucial for constructing sound definitions in Islamic logic, jurisprudence, and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term to be defined
Start with a concrete term from an Islamic science, e.g., 'insān' (human), 'īmān' (faith), or 'salah' (prayer). The term is the object of definition.
2
Apply the wad' method: check convention
Determine if there is a linguistic, legal, or customary convention (wad') that specifies what the term was coined for. For example, linguists tell us 'insān' was coined for 'rational animal'. The mental parts of that concept (e.g., 'animal' and 'rational') are essential.
If no convention exists, proceed to the i'tibar method.
3
Extract the mental parts from the convention
From the conventional definition, list all the parts that constitute the concept. These are the essential (dhati) attributes. Anything not included in that list is accidental ('aradi).
For 'insān': 'animal' and 'rational' are essential; 'laughing' and 'writing' are accidental.
4
Apply the i'tibar method: rational consideration
If no clear convention exists, use intellectual consideration (i'tibar). Reflect on the external instance of the thing and identify which attributes are constitutive (qiwam) of its essence. Those are essential; others are accidental.
For example, for a human, consider that it is a body, sentient, moving by will, and rational potentially – these are constitutive, so essential.
5
Distinguish and verify
Compile the list of essential attributes from either method. Verify that removing any of them would destroy the concept. Attributes whose removal does not affect the concept are accidental.
This step confirms the separation of dhati from 'aradi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَقَدْ ذَكَرَ القَرَافِيُّ تَبِعَهُ عَلَى ذَلِكَ الزَّركَشِيُّ وَغَيْرُهُ وَعَلَيْهِ فَلَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ لِلْوَضْعِ دَخْلٌ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الدَّاخِلِ مِنَ الْخَارِجِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ فَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى فَرْضِ الْعَقْلِ. قوله : (بِأَحَدٍ طَرِيقَيْنِ) مَرْجِعُهُمَا : إِمَّا الْوَضْعُ وَإِمَّا الاعْتِبَارُ، فَإِذَا ثَبَتَ فِي لَفْظٍ أَنَّهُ وُضِعَ لِمَفْهُومٍ فَمَا احْتَوَى عَلَيْهِ ذَلِكَ الْمَفْهُومُ مِنَ الأَجْزَاءِ الْعَقْلِيَّةِ ذَاتِيّ لَهُ وَمَا سِوَاهُ عَرَضِيّ؛ سَوَاءً كَانَ الْوَضْعُ لُغَوِيًّا أَوْ شَرْعِيًّا أَوْ عُرْفِيًّا. فَاللُّغَوِيّ ك: «الإِنْسَانُ» مَثَلًا ثَبَتَ بِنَقْلِ أَهْلِ اللُّغَةِ أَنَّهُ وُضِعَ لِمَفْهُومِ «الحَيَوَان النَّاطِق»، فَنَعْلَمُ أَنَّ كُلَّا مِنْ هَذَيْنِ ذَاتِيّ لَهُ، وَمَا سِوَاهُمَا مِنَ الضَّاحِكِ، وَالْكَاتِبِ، وَنَحْوِهِمَا عَرَضَيٌّ لَهُ؛ لِخُرُوجِهِمَا عَمَّا وُضِعَ لَهُ اللَّفْظُ. وَالشَّرْعِيُّ كَ: «الإِيمَانُ» مَثَلًا ثَبَتَ بِاسْتِقْرَاءِ الشَّرِيعَةِ أَنَّهُ وُضِعَ: لِلتَّصْدِيقِ بِمَا جَاءَ بِهِ الرَّسُولُ فَنَعْلَمُ أَنَّ كُلَّا مِنَ التَّصْدِيقِ، وَكَوْنِهِ بِمَا جَاءَ بِهِ الرَّسُولُ ذَاتِيّ لَهُ، وَمَا سِوَى ذَلِكَ مِنْ: كَوْنِ الإِيمَانِ عَاصِمًا، وَمُنْجِيًا مِنَ الْخُلُودِ فِي النَّارِ وَنَحْوِهِمَا عَرَضَيٌّ لَهُ، وَنَظِيرُهُ: الصَّلَاةُ لِلْأَرْكَانِ الْمَخْصُوصَةِ. وَالْعُرْفِيُّ كَ: «الحال» ثَبَتَ فِي عُرْفِ النُّحَاةِ أَنَّهُ: «مَوْضُوعٌ لِلْوَصْفِ الْفَضْلَةِ الْمُبَيِّنِ لِلْهَيْئَةِ، فَنَعْلَمُ أَنَّ كُلَّا مِنْ هَذِهِ الأَجْزَاءِ ذَاتِيّ وَمَا سِوَاهَا عَرَضِيٌّ كََكَوْنِهِ مَنْصُوبًا أَوْ مُفْرَدًا، أَوْ جُمْلَةً، أَوْ مُنْكَرًا). وَأَمَّا الاعْتِبَارُ فَأَنْ تَنْظُرَ فِي مَفْهُومِ شَيْءٍ وَتَعْتَبِرَهُ بِاعْتِبَارَاتٍ وَتَعْزِلَ مِنْهَا مَا تَرَاهُ قِوَامًا لِذَلِكَ الشَّيْءِ فَيَكُونُ ذَاتِيًّا ، وَمَا بَقِيَ عَرَضِيٌّ؛ كَأَنْ تَنْظُرَ فِي الإِنْسَانِ الْخَارِجِيِّ فَتَعْتَبِرَ لَهُ مِنَ الأَوْصَافِ: أَنَّهُ جِسْمٌ، وَأَنَّهُ حَسَّاسٌ مُتَحَرِّكٌ بِالإِرَادَةِ مُتَفَكِّرٌ بِالْقُوَّةِ، فَهِيَ قِوَامُُ فَهِيَ ذَاتِيَّةٌ، وَغَيْرُهَا كَ: «الضَّحِكِ» عَرَضِيٌّ.
His saying: 'And al-Qarafi mentioned' — al-Zarkashi and others followed him on that. According to this, it is necessary that usage (wad') has a role in defining what is internal from what is external; if not, then it reverts to mental hypothesis (farḍ al-'aql). His saying: 'by one of two ways' — their source is either usage or consideration (i'tibar). So if it is established about a word that it was coined for a concept, then whatever that concept contains of mental parts is essential (dhati) for it, and what is other than it is accidental ('aradi); whether the usage is linguistic, legal, or customary. The linguistic, like 'insan' for example, is established by the transmission of linguists that it was coined for the concept 'rational animal', so we know that each of these two is essential for it, and what is other than them, like 'laughing', 'writing', and the like, is accidental for it, because they fall outside what the word was coined for. The legal, like 'iman' (faith) for example, is established by induction of the Shari'ah that it was coined for 'belief in what the Messenger brought', so we know that each of 'belief' and 'being in what the Messenger brought' is essential for it, and what is other than that, like 'faith being protective' and 'saving from eternal fire' and the like, is accidental for it. Similarly, prayer (salah) for its specific pillars. The customary, like 'hal' (state/condition), established in the custom of grammarians that it is 'coined for the superfluous description clarifying the state', so we know that each of these parts is essential and what is other than it is accidental, like being accusative, singular, a sentence, or indefinite. As for consideration (i'tibar), it is that you look at the concept of a thing and consider it from different aspects, and separate out from them what you see as the constitution of that thing, so it is essential, and what remains is accidental; like if you look at external human and consider for it among the attributes: that it is a body, that it is sentient, moving by will, rational potentially, then these are its constitution, so they are essential, and others like 'laughing' are accidental.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage is a sharh (commentary) that expands on a matn statement about al-Qarafi's two ways to identify essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) attributes. The commentator first notes that al-Zarkashi and others followed al-Qarafi, and asserts that the role of linguistic/legal/customary usage (wad') is necessary in distinguishing what is internal (essential) from what is external (accidental). If no such usage exists, then the mind (farḍ al-'aql) takes over. He then explains the two ways: the first is through usage, which can be linguistic, legal, or customary. He provides three examples: linguistic: 'insan' is coined for 'rational animal', so both parts are essential; legal: 'iman' is coined for 'belief in what the Messenger brought', so its parts (belief and the object of belief) are essential; customary: 'hal' in grammar is coined for 'a superfluous description clarifying state', so its parts are essential. The second way is intellectual consideration (i'tibar): one examines a concept and identifies the attributes that constitute its essence (qiwam); those are essential, while others are accidental. He illustrates this with the human: the attributes of body, sentience, voluntary motion, and rational potential constitute the essence, so they are essential; laughter is accidental. This passage thoroughly defines how to determine essentiality in definitions, emphasizing that both conventional meaning and rational analysis play a role.

Key Terms
ذاتي
عرضي
وضع
اعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Relativity of Essential and Accidental
Author illustrates relativity of essential/accidental with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why essential/accidental is relative and provide examples from the five universals.

Core Idea
Essential and accidental are not fixed; they depend on which essence the attribute is related to.

In the previous lesson, you learned the distinction between essential and accidental. Here, the commentator emphasizes that this distinction is not absolute but relative: the same attribute can be essential for one thing and accidental for another. Understanding this relativity is crucial for constructing accurate definitions in logic and the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The commentator states: 'Whether a thing is essential or accidental is relative.' Find the Arabic sentence that expresses this.
Look for the phrase 'أَمْرٌ نِسْبِيٌّ يَخْتَلِفُ بِالإِضَافَةِ'.
2
Examine the first example
The example: 'animal' is essential for 'man' but accidental for 'natiq' (rational). Notice why: animal is part of man's essence but not part of natiq's essence.
Natiq here is considered a separate essence (the rational faculty).
3
Examine the second example
Conversely, 'natiq' is essential for 'man' but accidental for 'animal'. Check the reasoning: natiq is part of man's definition but not part of animal's.
This shows that the same attribute can switch status depending on the relation.
4
Generalize the principle
The commentator concludes: 'And on this, make an analogy.' This means you should apply this relative thinking to any attribute when defining something.
If you do not grasp this relativity, you might mistakenly treat an accidental attribute as essential in a definition.

الثَّانِي : يُعْلَمُ مِمَّا تَقَدَّمَ أَنَّ كَوْنَ الشَّيْءِ ذَاتِيًّا أَوْ عَرَضِيًّا أَمْرٌ نِسْبِيٌّ يَخْتَلِفُ بِالإِضَافَةِ؛ مَثَلاً : الْحَيَوَانُ ذَاتِيُّ لِلإِنْسَانِ؛ لِدُخُولِهِ فِي مَاهِيَّتِهِ، وَعَرَضَيٌّ لِلنَّاطِقِ لِخُرُوجِهِ عَنْ مَاهِيَّتِهِ ، فَهُوَ عَرَضٌ لَهُ عَامٌ . وَالنَّاطِقُ ذَاتِيٌّ لِلإِنْسَانِ كَمَا مَرَّ ، وَعَرَضِيُّ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِلحَيَوَانِ لِخُرُوجِهِ عَنْ مَاهِيَّتِهِ ، فَهُوَ خَاصَّةٌ لَهُ غَيْرُ شَامِلَةٍ ، وَعَلَى هَذَا فَقِسْ. قوله : (وَعَرَضَيٌّ لِلنَّاطِقِ) أي: لأنَّ المراد بـ «النَّاطق» هو المتفكر في العلوم، والحَيَوَان خارج عنه. قوله : (وَالنَّاطِقُ ذَاتِيُّ لِلإِنْسَانِ ما ذكره مِن أنَّ الناطق فصل ذاتي للإنسان؛ أي: وكذا الصَّاهل للفرس، والنَّاهق للحمار ؛ إنَّما يأتي على قول الحكماء والغزالي والحليمي . أمَّا جمهور المتكلمين فالأجسام عندهم كلُّها متماثلة في الماهية لا تختلف إلَّا بالعوارض، وأنَّ النَّاطقيَّة وغيرها عوارض ، ولذلك صح مسخ الإنسان قرداً ،ونحوه، وإلى هذا ذهب الشيخ المنجور وهو الحقُّ؛ لأنَّ الرُّوح على مذهب أهل الحقِّ جوهر، وحقيقة الإنسان مثلاً تركبت من الجواهر الفردة والجواهر كلُّها متماثلة بالحقيقة؛ إذ الجوهر عبارة عن المتحيز بذاته، وهذا المفهوم واحد في جميعها ، فلا تختلف إلَّا بالعوارض . ومذهب الحكماء ومن وافقهم : أنَّ الإنسان ليس محض الجرم، بل فيه المجرَّد وهو الرُّوح، ولا يلزم من تماثل الأجرام تماثل الحقائق، وإلى هذا ذهب الشَّيخ القصَّار، والمجرَّد عندهم ما ليس بجرم ولا عَرَض؛ أي: ليس متحيّزاً ولا قائماً بمتحيز، وجعلوا منه النفوس والعقول والملائكة. قوله: (وَعَرَضِيٌّ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِلحَيَوَانِ) أي : لأنَّ الحَيَوَان هو الجسمُ النَّامي الحسّاس المتحرك بالإرادة، فالنَّاطِقُ خارج عن حقيقته.
The second: It is known from what preceded that a thing being essential or accidental is a relative matter, differing depending on the relation. For example: animal (hayawan) is essential for man (insan) because it enters into his essence, and accidental for rational (natiq) because it is outside its essence, so it is a general accident for it. And rational is essential for man as passed, and accidental in relation to animal, because it is outside its essence, so it is a special non-inclusive property for it. And on this, make an analogy. His saying: '(and accidental for natiq)' meaning: because what is meant by 'natiq' is the one who thinks in sciences, and animal is outside of it. His saying: '(and natiq is essential for man)' - what he mentioned that natiq is an essential differentia for man; i.e., similarly the neighing for the horse, the braying for the donkey; only comes according to the saying of the philosophers, al-Ghazali and al-Halimi. As for the majority of the mutakallimun, all bodies according to them are similar in essence, not differing except by accidents, and that natiqiyya and others are accidents. Therefore, the metamorphosis (maskh) of a man into a monkey and the like is deemed possible. And to this went Shaykh al-Manjur and it is the truth; because the soul according to the ahl al-haqq is a substance, and the reality of man for example is composed of individual substances (jawahir farda), and all substances are identical in reality; since substance is defined as that which is self-subsistent (mutahayyiz bi-dhatihi), and this concept is one in all of them, so they do not differ except by accidents. And the school of the philosophers and those who agree with them: that man is not merely a body, but in him there is the abstract (mujarrad) which is the soul, and the similarity of bodies does not necessitate similarity of realities. And to this went Shaykh al-Qassar. And the abstract according to them is whatever is not a body nor an accident; i.e., not self-subsistent nor subsisting in a self-subsistent. And they considered among it the souls, intellects, and angels. His saying: '(and accidental in relation to animal)' i.e., because animal is the growing, sentient, moving by will body, so natiq is outside its reality.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the earlier claim that natiq is essential for man, and it also demonstrates the relativity of essential/accidental. The commentator begins by stating that whether an attribute is essential or accidental depends on the thing it is attributed to; it is relative. For example, 'animal' is essential for 'man' but accidental for 'natiq' (rational). Similarly, 'natiq' is essential for 'man' but accidental for 'animal'. The commentator then elaborates on the second example with a marginal note: by 'natiq' is meant the one who thinks in sciences; animal is outside of that. Then he introduces a major dispute: the claim that natiq is an essential differentia (fasl dhati) for man is only accepted by the philosophers (like Aristotle's school), al-Ghazali, and al-Halimi. The majority of the mutakallimun (Islamic theologians) hold that all bodies are identical in essence; they differ only by accidents. Therefore, natiqiyya (rationality) is an accident, not essential. They support this with the possibility of metamorphosis (maskh) — a human can become a monkey, which would be impossible if rationality were essential. The commentator sides with the mutakallimun, citing Shaykh al-Manjur, and argues that the soul is a substance (jawhar) and all substances are alike, differing only by accidents. The philosophers' view, that man has an abstract soul separate from the body, is presented but not endorsed. Finally, he returns to clarify that 'natiq' is accidental to 'animal' because animal's essence is a growing, sentient, moving body, and rationality is outside that. This passage refines the definition by acknowledging the theological perspective and the relativity of essential properties.

ذاتي
عرضي
ماهية

B) Accidental, because animal is outside the essence of rational
C) Both essential and accidental
D) Neither essential nor accidental

Key Takeaway
Whether an attribute is essential or accidental is relative; the same attribute can be essential for one thing and accidental for another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Natiq Dispute: Philosophers vs. Mutakallimun
Author critiques the view that natiq is essential, presenting the mutakallimun's alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dispute over whether natiq is essential or accidental for man and evaluate the arguments of both sides.

Core Idea
Whether natiq is essential depends on one's metaphysical commitment about the soul.

After establishing the relativity of essential/accidental, the commentator introduces a major dispute: Is natiq (rational faculty) an essential differentia for man? The philosophers say yes, but the majority of mutakallimun say no. This dispute has profound implications for definitions in kalam and fiqh. Let us analyze the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two camps
Find the two groups mentioned: al-hukama' (philosophers, al-Ghazali, al-Halimi) and jumhur al-mutakallimun (the majority of theologians).
The commentator sides with the mutakallimun.
2
Summarize the philosophers' view
The philosophers hold that man has an abstract soul (al-mujarrad) which is not a body or accident, and natiq is an essential differentia.
They argue that similarity of bodies does not imply similarity of realities.
3
Summarize the mutakallimun's view
The mutakallimun hold that all bodies are identical in essence (muttamāthila fī al-māhiyya) and differ only by accidents. Therefore, natiq is an accident.
They support this with the possibility of maskh (metamorphosis): a man can become a monkey.
4
Evaluate the evidence
The commentator presents maskh as evidence: if natiq were essential, metamorphosis would change the essence, which is impossible. Thus, natiq must be accidental.
This argument depends on the Ash'ari metaphysical framework.
5
Assess the author's position
The commentator explicitly sides with the mutakallimun, calling it 'al-haqq' (the truth), citing Shaykh al-Manjur.
He adds that the soul is a substance (jawhar) and all substances are alike, so differences are accidental.
